# Creating Accessible PDFs

> Accessibility means making sure your content is available to as many people as possible. When you make your PDFs accessible, it means adding tags, bookmarks, alt text, and other information that makes the files readable to users of assistive technology. It's now much easier to use Microsoft Word and Adobe InDesign to create valid, accessible PDF files. In this course, instructor Chad Chelius expla

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392) | 8h 16m | Intermediate | 12K learners

### Introduction

#### [The ultimate guide to accessible PDFs](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/the-ultimate-guide-to-accessible-pdfs-25575414?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/the-ultimate-guide-to-accessible-pdfs-25575414?u=76281980&t=0)** - Some people think that document accessibility is hard, but really it's not. Now in this course, I take you down several different paths depending on your situation and show you how to make an existing PDF file accessible, how to make [[Microsoft Office|Office]] applications accessible, and my personal favorite, how to make accessible PDFs from Adobe InDesign. Hi there, my name is Chad Chelius, and for the last 15 years, I've been working in the field of document accessibility. Making documents accessible is something that I'm incredibly passionate about. That's why I created my company, Chax Training and Consulting, to help users to create accessible PDF files and supporting them throughout that process. Without further ado, let's get started.

#### [Introduction to creating accessible PDFs](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/introduction-to-creating-accessible-pdfs?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/introduction-to-creating-accessible-pdfs?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this course, I'll be showing you how to create accessible PDF files from a number of different applications. First off, I'll be showing you how to take an existing PDF file and make it fully accessible. To do this, I'll be using an application called Adobe Acrobat Pro DC. Adobe Acrobat Pro DC is the subscription version of Adobe Acrobat that requires a monthly subscription to use the product. This version of Adobe Acrobat is what Adobe often refers to as the continuous release of Acrobat, because it's continually being updated periodically with new features. You will need the Pro version of Adobe Acrobat to follow along as the standard version does not contain the accessibility features that we'll be using to remediate PDF files. Now there's another version of Adobe Acrobat Pro that some organizations choose to purchase, which is called Adobe Acrobat Pro 2020. This version of Adobe Acrobat is the perpetual version of the product, which means it is not subscription-based. This product functions very similar to Adobe Acrobat Pro DC, except the product is never updated with new features like Adobe Acrobat Pro DC is. For this reason, it's possible to experience some slight differences between the [[Microsoft Products|products]] as you follow along. From there, we'll move on to learning how to create accessible PDF files from the [[Microsoft Office]] applications, namely [[Microsoft Word]] and [[Microsoft PowerPoint]].
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/introduction-to-creating-accessible-pdfs?u=76281980&t=97)** The version of the [[Microsoft Office|Office]] applications that I'm using in this course are the versions that ship with the Office 365 subscription. [[Microsoft Word|Word]] and [[Microsoft PowerPoint|PowerPoint]] 2016 work as well, as does Word and PowerPoint 2010. However, the 2010 version of the products had some limitations that you will certainly experience and notice if you're trying to follow along in this course. Finally, we'll be learning how to create accessible PDF files from Adobe InDesign. For this course, I'm going to be using the current version of Adobe InDesign, which is InDesign 2021. There have been some updates and improvements to features in InDesign in the current version, including some related to accessibility. If you're using an older version of Adobe InDesign, you should be fine, but it is possible that you could see a slight difference here and there. To make it as easy as possible for your copy of the InDesign file to look like mine, I've leveraged Adobe Fonts, which are online fonts that you can activate on your computer free of charge as part of your Creative Cloud subscription. Font licensing makes it difficult to share fonts freely with other users, so Adobe Fonts alleviates that problem. Now, some users may not have access to the Creative Cloud features and therefore may not be able to activate the fonts that I've used. Don't worry though. You're not out of luck. Simply choose type, find replace fonts on your computer. And this will bring up the find replace fonts dialog box.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/introduction-to-creating-accessible-pdfs?u=76281980&t=195)** What you can do is select the missing font and then choose an appropriate replacement font down at the bottom. When you do, don't forget to enable the redefined style when changing all checkbox to update the appearance of the styles that use that font within that document. Doing this will allow you to follow along with me, although your documents certainly will look slightly different. If you're not running the latest versions of the applications that I've just described, which can happen for a variety of different reasons, you're not out of luck. I have several other PDF accessibility courses on [[LinkedIn]] Learning that cover older versions of products for creating accessible PDF files. If you're in this situation, simply head on over to [linkedin.com/learning/instructors/chad](https://linkedin.com/learning/instructors/chad)- chelius. There, you can peruse the other PDF accessibility courses as well as others that will help you on your way to learning PDF accessibility. Most of the videos that I've created for this course are done using the [[Windows]] operating system. I've done this for several reasons. First, most users who are tasked with creating accessible PDF files tend to be on the Windows platform. The significance of the Windows platform isn't as important for the InDesign videos as InDesign is exactly the same on both platforms. Unfortunately, this is not the case for the Office products. Word and PowerPoint can have significant differences from one operating system to the other.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/introduction-to-creating-accessible-pdfs?u=76281980&t=289)** And to be fair, the features for creating accessible PDFs on Windows is more robust than they are on the Mac platform, although you can still create accessible PDF files on the Mac OS as well.

### 1. Understanding Accessibility

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [What is accessibility?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/what-is-accessibility?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/what-is-accessibility?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] So what exactly is accessibility? Well, accessibility by definition is making something able to be used, entered, or reached, making something easy to approach, reach, enter, speak with, or use, or making something suitable for disabled people to reach, enter, or use as a result of design modifications. When you start to look around you, you'll see examples of accessibility everywhere. Ramps, for example, allow users to more easily get to another location without having to navigate stairs. Another great example is curb ramps. In the last couple of decades, we've seen curb ramps appear all over the world. We see Braille buttons in places like elevators and restroom signs to help visually impaired users find their way. And one of the best examples I've seen is the European Euro note. Each denomination is a different size with a tactile feel to help visually impaired users identify which bill they have in their hand. This is amazing, and it really makes me consider the situation in my country and many other countries and the experience that visually impaired users have when trying to pay for goods. What I like to remind people of is the fact that accessibility benefits everyone. Ramps aren't just for disabled people. The elderly appreciate ramps because they make it easier to access areas. Curb ramps aren't just for people using wheelchairs. Parents pushing strollers
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/what-is-accessibility?u=76281980&t=94)** and delivery people delivering goods on carts also benefit and appreciate curb ramps. Many of us end up being temporarily disabled due to an injury, and therefore also benefit from ramps and other accessibility features. In the context of this course, we're going to be talking about document accessibility, specifically, electronic documents, more specifically, PDF documents. There are special requirements needed to make documents accessible. I'd like to use an analogy here. When you pick up a newspaper to read it, what's the first thing you do? Right, you scan the headlines. Well, we need to provide that same capability when a user of assistive technology reads an electronic document by adding heading structure to that document. When proper heading structure is not added to a document or the document is not tagged at all, it's the equivalent of buying a newspaper at the newsstand, getting it home, and finding that all of the text is in 12-point type with no headings at all, or worse, that the document is completely blank. That wouldn't be acceptable to anyone, and nor should it be. In this course, we're going to focus on PDF document accessibility. We'll be adding functionality to the document to facilitate the making of an accessible PDF file to provide a rich reading experience to users of assistive technology. With all that being said, let's dive in and learn how to make PDF documents that are accessible to all users.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

#### [Accessibility standards](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/accessibility-standards?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/accessibility-standards?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] I'd like to talk for a minute about accessibility standards. Standards are important as they give us a target to shoot for and ensures that everyone is trying to achieve the same goal. Without standards, it's a bit like the Wild West with everybody doing their own thing. If you're old enough to remember the World Wide Web, what it was like back in the early 1990s, you'll appreciate that reference. Now, in this course, we're focusing on making PDF documents accessible. We'll be focused on the current standards that apply to the accessible PDF format. The first standard, which is WCAG is the Web Content Accessibility Guidelines produced by the World Wide Web consortium and is widely recognized standard for PDF as well as [[Web Accessibility]]. It contains almost 100 different success criteria required for accessibility. PDF/UA, which stands for Universal Accessibility is an ISO standard that defines how accessible PDF documents should be structured. It applies to document creators, but also software developers to ensure a unified approach to document accessibility. Now, several laws such as section 508 in the U.S, AODA in Canada, EN 301549 in Europe and DDA in Australia, have made it law that electronic documents need to be accessible and most referenced the WCAG standard. Now some agencies may do a variation on one of the standards. For example, here in the U.S the Department of Health
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/accessibility-standards?u=76281980&t=93)** and Human Services has their own standard. That is mostly based on WCAG with a few twists. So always do your research and find out what the desired standard should be for your documents.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Web Accessibility]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** pdf (5), wcag (3), iso (1), aoda (1), dda (1)
> **Definitions:** stands for (1), is an  (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (1), for example (1)
> **CLI Commands:** find (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Check PDF accessibility](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/check-pdf-accessibility-25577333?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/check-pdf-accessibility-25577333?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] So how do we really know when a PDF file is accessible? There's a certain degree of PDF accessibility that relies on a human, such as you or me, to walk the tags tree of a document to ensure that content is tagged correctly and that the tags are in the correct order. And that's the really important step, but we also need a tool that can dive into our file and identify any issues in an automated fashion to avoid us as humans from having to go through every single task step by step. For this task, we require PDF accessibility checkers to evaluate our documents and let us know its status. So let's start with the first step that I mentioned, which is to walk the tags tree. And this is a term that you're going to hear me mention more than once throughout this course. And to walk the tags tree, the first thing we want to do is open up our tags pane. So to do that, I'm going to open up my tags pane. So I'm basically going to come over here to the right, I'm going to right click on a blank area here, and I'm going to choose accessibility tags. And what this is going to do is it's going to open up my tags pane. And walking the tags tree is really a pretty simple task. You basically click on the first tag, and now using the down arrow or up arrow key on my keyboard, I can walk the tags tree from one tag to the next. And as I do this, you're going to notice that it's going to show me the object highlighting on the page, right? So that creates the connection of the content on the page with the tag that I currently have selected.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/check-pdf-accessibility-25577333?u=76281980&t=94)** Now as I'm doing this, I'm going to look at the tag. And so here when I get to the next tag, here's a big heading, and I want that to be tagged as an H1. And as you can see, it is in fact tagged as an H1. Now I go to my next tag, that's my table of contents, that looks pretty good. Go to my next tag, that's an H1. And as I'm going down through my tags, I'm really just kind of evaluating the content. Again, one, making sure that everything is tagged correctly, and two, making sure that everything is tagged in the correct order. And that satisfies two very important WCAG success criteria, which is info and relationships, as well as meaningful sequence. And that's really what walking the tags tree is doing for me. So, I'm not going to make you watch me walk the tag tree in this entire document. I think you probably understand now what the process entails. So I'm going to go ahead and hide my tags pane. And the next thing I'm going to do is I'm going to evaluate my document, and I'm going to start by doing this using the Acrobat Checker. Now I can do that in the upper left corner of my screen, I'm going to click on All tools. And then you may have to scroll through here, but I want to find the tool that says Prepare for accessibility. And within the Prepare for Accessibility tool, you're going to see an option called Check for accessibility. I'm going to choose that option,
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/check-pdf-accessibility-25577333?u=76281980&t=188)** and that's going to bring up my Accessibility Checker options. I want to make sure that I'm evaluating the entire document, right? You can see that I can evaluate various parts of the document if I want, but I want to evaluate the entire document, and I'm going to click the Start Checking button. Now, what's going to happen over here on the right, I'm going to get the results of that check. And you can see that Acrobat is evaluating my content in seven different categories. You can see that it's evaluating my document, the page content, [[Forms]], alternate text, tables, lists, and headings. Now, you're only going to see an issue if one exists. And all of these categories, from page content down to headings, there are no issues. This document is in pretty good shape. But in the document category, you're going to notice that there's two issues. I'm not going to call them errors. They're really just warnings letting you know that you as a human being need to check these two categories. And those two categories are color contrast as well as logical reading order. And again, the logical reading order we're evaluating as we're walking the tags tree. So if you perform that step, you're pretty much good to go. And if it makes you feel any better, you can right click on that warning and choose Pass, and that will now prevent it from showing up as an issue. The color contrast, again, that's a task
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/check-pdf-accessibility-25577333?u=76281980&t=281)** that you as a human need to evaluate and make sure that all of the text and graphics in your document meets minimum color contrast according to the WCAG requirements. Now the thing about the Acrobat Checker is that although it does a fairly good job of checking our document for issues, it's really just checking our document for significant barriers. The Acrobat Checker does not actually check your document against any standard. And when it comes to PDF documents, there's two standards by which we can gauge the compliance of our document, and that's the PDF/UA standard, as well as the WCAG standard. And the Acrobat Checker unfortunately does not check either one of our documents. So I'm going to go ahead and open a checker that I highly recommend, and this checker is called the PAC 2024 Checker. So what I'm going to do here, we could either click the Open Document button and navigate our hard drive for the document. I prefer to drag and drop. So what I'm going to do is I'm going to grab this file, which is the 02_03 file. I'm going to drag this and drop it onto this icon right here. And when I do that, the PAC 2024 Checker is going to evaluate that document, okay? Now I've already remediated this file, right? So I've already done everything to this file, and that's why I'm getting all green check marks. You likely will not get all green check marks right off the spot like this.
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/check-pdf-accessibility-25577333?u=76281980&t=375)** You're going to have a couple of errors that you're going to have to address and figure out what the problem is. But as you can see here, the PAC Checker runs my file through a number of different categories, way more categories than we had in Acrobat. And you could see some of these categories under Content. There's 12,000 items that it's evaluating, which is pretty impressive. But anyway, the PAC Checker is really pretty impressive because what I love about the PAC Checker way down here at the bottom, you can click on the Results in detail button. And when I do that, it's going to actually bring up all of these categories, and I can dive in to each one of these categories and I can evaluate what that category is or what the issue is, right? So when you get errors, you can dive into that error and it's going to tell you exactly what the problem is. So the PAC Checker's really quite informative. Now, the other nice thing you can see up here in my tabs, this is passing the PDF/UA standard. It's also passing the WCAG 2.1 standard. So, both of those standards are compliant. And for both of those standards, I can click on the PDF report button, and that will actually generate a report for me. I'm just going to put this on my desktop for now. This generates a really nicely formatted report that kind of tells me how compliant the document is and if there are in fact any issues.
>
> **[7:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/check-pdf-accessibility-25577333?u=76281980&t=468)** And this is a great report that you can send to your client to kind of confirm that your document meets the specifications of that standard. Now, in addition to that, you'll notice there's a couple of icons over here to the right. If you click on the Logical structure button, it's going to show you all of the tags that are in your document, which is really quite cool. These are all the tags in my document. One thing about this is that it's actually not showing you the role map. So I know you're probably looking for H1, H2, H3. It's not actually showing you that because the PAC Checker ignores the role map when it's showing you these tags. But keep in mind the role map is applying the proper tag. And if you click on the Artifacts tab, you could see every item on each page that is currently being artifacted, which is pretty cool. Now in addition, this button will give you your document [[Statistics]]. It tells you how many caption tags you have, how many figure tags, how many list tags, which is really pretty amazing. And then finally, one of my favorite features is we have this third icon, which is the screen reader preview. And the screen reader preview basically visually shows you how the document is tagged. So, you can see here the contents is tagged with an H1 tag. All of this is tagged with the TOC tag. As we go down a little bit further,
>
> **[9:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/check-pdf-accessibility-25577333?u=76281980&t=560)** this is tagged with the H2 tag. So it's basically visually representing what a screen reader is reading to you, and it's a great way to kind of visually identify how the content in your document is tagged. So, that's basically what we need to do to ensure that our document is compliant by leveraging robust checkers. Again, the PAC 2024 Checker is one of them. You can also use the Common Look Validator, and there are other applications such as GrackleDocs and PDFix that have built -in checkers to help you do the same thing that we've done here.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

Doing so gives you a true experience of how someone using assistive technology will experience reading the document. #### [Acrobat Pro DC setup and workspace](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/acrobat-pro-dc-setup-and-workspace-25576403?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/acrobat-pro-dc-setup-and-workspace-25576403?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In the latest release of Adobe Acrobat, Adobe decided to revamp the Acrobat interface and pretty drastically change things from how Acrobat used to look. Now whether you like this or whether you don't like it, the reality is that when you install Acrobat in its current form, you're going to open up Acrobat and you're going to be like, "Wait a minute, where did everything go?" Now, what I want to show you right now, currently I'm viewing Acrobat using the new interface. What I want to show you is if you come up here to the Menu button in the upper left hand corner, you'll notice that there's an option in here called Disable new Acrobat. And if you choose that, it's going to make you restart Acrobat. But when you do it, now you're going to be back to the old Acrobat interface. Now what I'm going to do in this video is I'm going to show you how to configure the Acrobat interface using both configurations because I realize that for many of you out there, you may prefer one interface over the other, and that's completely fine. There's nothing at all wrong with that. What I do want to make you aware of, however, is that I have it on pretty good accord that in future versions of Acrobat, you're not going to be able to disable the new interface.

You know, it's a tool that I use all the time, so I like it to be one of the topmost tools in my list. But now I'm going to click on that Accessibility tool,
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/acrobat-pro-dc-setup-and-workspace-25576403?u=76281980&t=193)** and now that that's there, I have access to all of my accessibility options within the Accessibility tool. So by adding it to your shortcuts over here to the right, you have quick access to it, and that'll help you when working with PDF files that you're trying to make accessible. Now what I'm also going to do is over here on the left, I'm going to click on this little arrow about middle way down on my screen, and there's a couple of panels that you're going to want to make available. So if you right click on this blank area here, what you want to do is choose Accessibility tags, that's one panel that we want to use. We're going to right click again and we're going to choose Content. And then we're going to right click one more time and we're going to choose Order. So those are like the three panels that you could potentially need to access as you're working with your PDF files. Now let's see how to do that with the new Acrobat interface. So to kind of get back to the new interface, I'm going to go to my View menu up here, and I'm going to choose Enable new Acrobat. Again, I'm going to have to restart Acrobat, but that's now going to take me into the new Acrobat interface with my document open. And as you can see now, over here on the right, all of the tools that I previously added on the left side are now open over here to the right.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/acrobat-pro-dc-setup-and-workspace-25576403?u=76281980&t=288)** But to make them available, you would do the same thing, just kind of right click on a blank area, and you would choose Accessibility tags, right click again and choose Content, and then right click again and choose Order. And as you can see here, I have my content, my order, and my tags available over here on the right-hand side. Over here on the left-hand side, to access your Accessibility tool, you'll simply click on the All tools button at the very top. And in this particular interface, to see all of your tools, you can just click on the View more link, and that's going to show you all of the tools that are available. Now, what's interesting about the new interface is that I can click on Prepare for Accessibility, and I can drag it to rearrange it. It's a lot easier to do than it was in the old interface. So I can move this up, and I'm just going to drag this all the way up to the top because that's where I want it to go. And now when I click on Prepare for Accessibility, you're going to see that all of the tools within the Prepare for Accessibility are available for you to use. So that's essentially how to configure your various workspaces in both the old Acrobat workspace and the new Acrobat Workspace as well.

#### [PDF accessibility requirements](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/pdf-accessibility-requirements?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/pdf-accessibility-requirements?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Not all PDF files are created equal. This is important to understand. And although we're going to be relying on accessibility checkers to check our documents, we as humans need to impart our knowledge to ensure that a document is truly compliant. Let me show you a couple of examples here. In the chapter two folder, I have two files, one called 02_02_good and 02_02_bad. And what I'm going to do is I'm going to run both of these through the PAC checker, and you can see that the good file is fully compliant. And if I drag the bad file over here, you can see that the bad file is also fully compliant. Well, what makes one good, and what makes one bad? Well, I'm going to start by opening up the good file. And if I go to the tag structure here, you can see that my document contains heading levels indicating where there are headings. And you'll notice that I have a caption for the figure. And essentially my document, and specifically the content within the document, is tagged what we call semantically. Basically, the elements within the document are tagged appropriately. This is a heading, this is a caption, so on and so forth. Now, if I close this and I open up the bad version of this file, you can see that this document does not have any heading structure, right?
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/pdf-accessibility-requirements?u=76281980&t=95)** Although we do have some tags, none of my headings are identified appropriately. But notice that when I ran it through the checker, it passed with flying colors. And that's because what we need to remember is that checkers fundamentally are pretty dumb, right? A checker is not smart enough to know that text that is big in the document should in fact be tagged as a heading. It doesn't know that the text that appears underneath a figure should be tagged as a caption. And so you and I, as people who are remediating PDF documents, need to remember this. And although relying on a checker is very useful for a lot of things, you know, a checker is going to tell me if a figure is missing alternate text. It is going to tell me if there's an object in the document that is not tagged, right? So there are some fundamental things that a checker is very good for. But in the next chapter, I'm going to be talking about a task that we perform called walking the tags tree. And walking the tags tree serves several purposes, one of which is to make sure that everything is tagged, two is to make sure that everything is in the correct order, but three is to also make sure that the appropriate tag is identified in the content of the document. So as you can see from a distance, these files look the same.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/pdf-accessibility-requirements?u=76281980&t=189)** But a deeper dive revealed some problems that would, in fact, limit a user of assistive technology from being able to appropriately read that document. It's our job to make sure that our PDF document is tagged appropriately.

#### [The PDF remediation workflow](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/the-pdf-remediation-workflow?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/the-pdf-remediation-workflow?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this video, I wanted to talk for a few minutes about the PDF Remediation Workflow or at least about the possible workflow options that we have when our goal is to create an accessible PDF file. Now I've created this chart to kind of demonstrate the two main options that we have. Up here at the very top, you can see I'm starting with a source document, and it's important for us to understand that regardless of where your PDF or what PDF file you have open, it always came from somewhere else. It always came from some source document somewhere. And so, what I'm doing is I'm dividing the workflow into whether we're starting with the source document or we're not. So if we have the source document, the idea is, in this workflow, we add properties to the source file and that source file could be [[Microsoft Word]], [[Microsoft PowerPoint]], Adobe InDesign, whatever the source file is, some applications, such as the ones I listed, give us the ability to add attributes to that file to facilitate the export to an accessible PDF. So once you do that, you export to the PDF file, and now you have an accessible PDF. Now, if there are changes to that file, the benefit of this workflow is that we go back to the source file, we add the changes, we make the adjustments, and then we re-export the PDF, and all of those properties that had previously been added,
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/the-pdf-remediation-workflow?u=76281980&t=96)** still come over to the PDF and assist in making the document accessible. And then as soon as there are no changes, we have our final PDF. Now if you're not starting with a source document, which basically means we're starting with a PDF, we need to add the properties directly to the PDF file. Now as I'm pointing out here, this process is manual and it's time-consuming. And so, everything that we add into that will result in the accessible PDF. The problem is if there are no changes, well then, we have our final PDF and that's a best case scenario. But if there are changes, we now need to go back to the PDF and make adjustments. Or the worst situation is if somebody supplies us with an updated version of that PDF. And now, all those properties that we previously added, we need to re-add them into the new PDF. Then we go through the same process of the manual and time-consuming adjustments, and then, hopefully, we now no longer have any changes and we get to our final PDF. What you want to take away is that it's always better to start with the source application file versus the PDF. You can achieve an accessible PDF in both ways, but starting with a source file is going to give you a lot more flexibility, require a lot less effort
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/the-pdf-remediation-workflow?u=76281980&t=189)** and result in a really good accessible PDF document.

#### [Add metadata](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-metadata?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-metadata?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There is certain [[Metadata]] required in a PDF file in order to make a document accessible, specifically, a document title is required, and that title is required to be displayed when a PDF file is open. Although the document title is the only required metadata element in a PDF file, due to the fact that most PDF files will be posted online, it can be helpful to add other metadata to the file to assist in [[Search Engine Optimization (SEO)|search engine optimization]]. The title requirement for a PDF file can be found in the WCAG specifications under WCAG success criteria, 2.4.2 and it can be found in the PDF techniques for WCAG in item number 18. And finally, in the PDF/UA checkpoint, in checkpoint number six. To add the title to this document, because if you notice, currently in the tab for my document, and in the title bar, it's displaying the file name, and that of course is not very helpful. So to add a title to the document, I'm going to go to the file menu, and I'm going to choose properties. If you click on the description tab at the top, you're going to see the title field. So in the title field, I'm going to enter a relevant title. So here I'm going to type Two Trees Olive Oil, 2021 Employee Manual. And then the author of course,
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-metadata?u=76281980&t=94)** is going to be Two Trees Olive Oil. Now you can also enter a subject if you choose. So maybe I'll type in here, the 2021 Edition of the Two Trees Employee Manual. And then you can add some keywords if you wish. I might want to to put in here, say [[Human Resources (HR)|human resources]]. Two Trees. Employee. And then maybe manual. Now, that of course adds the required title that I'm looking for. But the secondary element here is that I need to come over here to the initial view tab, and in the show dropdown menu, we're going to change this from file name to document title. And when I click, okay, you're going to notice now in the title of the tab, and the document, that it is now displaying the title for the document, and no longer the file name. So adding the document title, and other relevant metadata to your file is an important step to making your PDF file accessible for all users.

#### [Tag content with the Reading Order tool](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tag-content-with-the-reading-order-tool?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tag-content-with-the-reading-order-tool?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Fundamentally, tags are how we identify the value of content in a document so that it can be read and interpreted using assistive technology. By applying a tag to an object, we're basically assigning semantic value to those elements. For example, a P tag is used to identify body text in a document, where an H1, H2, H3 tag and so on and so forth is used to identify heading tags in a document. Identifying heading tags is critical in helping users of assistive technology to disseminate information in the document and is covered by WCAG success criteria, 1.3.1, 2.4.1 and 2.4.6 It's also covered by the PDF techniques for WCAG in item number nine, as well as PDF/UA in checkpoint one. I'm going to begin by opening up the tags pane over here in my navigation pane. And what you're going to notice is that, it tells us that we currently have no tags available. Now, this is a bit of a problem because tags are the key element that we need to make our document accessible. So our job at this point in time is to tag the content in this document. The way that we're going to do that is we're going to come over here to our accessibility tool and I'm going to click on, the reading order tool. Now this is going to open up
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tag-content-with-the-reading-order-tool?u=76281980&t=92)** the reading order panel in my document and this is a key element that allows me to tag content in my document. The way that this works is I'm going to use my cursor and I'm going to click and drag around an object that I want to tag. And you'll notice when I let go, that object has been selected. Now, a little tip as you're doing this, when you're creating a selection around an object, go bigger than you think you need to. A common mistake I run into is people try to get really precise and they end up not selecting all of the elements. So as you watch me, and as I demonstrate this process, you're going to notice it whenever I select an object, I go way bigger than I need to. Now this item is going to be a figure, so I'm going to come up here and I'm going to click on the figure button and that is going to tag that object as a figure. I'm going to move this over to the right, so that you can see as I tag these items, you're going to see a new tag appear in the tags pane. So I'm going to go in and select this figure, again notice how I'm going bigger, I'm going to tag that as a figure, and then I'm going to come down here and select employee manual. Now, although this text is not super large, it's arguably very important information in the document and I'm going to tag that as a heading one. Now I'm going to select revised August 20th,
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tag-content-with-the-reading-order-tool?u=76281980&t=187)** and I'm going to tag that as text. Now, a lot of people ask me at this point, I'm going to go ahead and scroll down a little bit here so I can see what I'm working on, right? And a lot of people ask me, can I just select all four lines and tag them as text? The short answer is absolutely, that's totally going to work. My approach though, is that I try to tag my content semantically. I try to tag it basically based on the role that it serves. And in this particular case, each one of these lines, I'm going to tag as a separate paragraph. And a little trick here as you're doing this, notice how I'm overlapping the line above and below what I'm trying to tag and that helps me to select the object that I'm trying to tag. So I'm going to tag all four of these as text, and then we'll go ahead and move on to the next page. Now here, we have another main heading, so I'm going to select that and I'm going to tag that as another H1, because that's another main section of my document. Now here, we're getting into a table of contents, okay? And the table of contents kind of has a unique purpose and to be quite honest, it's challenging when we're tagging an existing PDF to get the behavior that we really want. So, what I'm going to do is I'm going to skip to page four
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tag-content-with-the-reading-order-tool?u=76281980&t=281)** and let's continue tagging from here. So section one introduction is going to be another heading, so I'm going to tag that as heading one. And then this content here is going to be texts, so up here, I have one paragraph, I'm going to select that, tag that as text. Now we get down here and this gets a little tricky. So what I'm going to do is I'm going to marquee this part of the text here and then what you can do is hold down the shift key on your keyboard and you can marquee the [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] of the text to add that to your selection. So I'm going to tag that as text, then I'll grab this next paragraph, tag that as text and then 1.1 changes in policy, I'm going to tag that as a heading too. Because it's a heading that is subordinate to this main heading here, but it's still a heading nonetheless. Then I'm going to tag the rest of these paragraphs as text and then up here, we have a figure with its caption. And what I'm going to do is I'm going to marquee that figure as well as its caption and in the reading order panel, I'm going to click on the figure caption button. Now I'm going to close that for a second and I'll show you that this last figure that I tagged, you'll notice that it instinctively has tagged the text as the caption, but the figure is tagged appropriately. So you can see here in the tagging structure,
>
> **[6:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tag-content-with-the-reading-order-tool?u=76281980&t=377)** all of the tags that I've created. Now, I'm not going to have you watch me tag the rest of the content but what I would like you to do is to continue on from here and go ahead and skip over bulleted or numbered lists as well as tables, but go ahead and tag the rest of the content in this document as practice and in the next video, we'll pick up from there.

#### [Dealing with content that spans more than one page](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/dealing-with-content-that-spans-more-than-one-page?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/dealing-with-content-that-spans-more-than-one-page?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] It's pretty normal to have textual content that flows from one page to another in a document. However, when tagging content using Adobe Acrobat, it's not possible to select content on more than one page at a time. Let's take a look at how to address this situation in this document. I'm just going to navigate through my pages until I find the first instance where a paragraph goes across the page. So in my case, that's page five. And you can see that the paragraph here kind of abruptly ends and then continues on page six. So let's take a look at what's going on there. I'm going to open my Tags pane and to find where the tag is for this content, what I can do is grab my selection tool and I'm just going to drag across some of that text. And if I come up here to the top of the Tags pane and click on this option button, I can choose Find Tag from Selection. And what that'll do is it'll highlight the content for the text that I previously had selected. Now, what we could do is we can collapse this. And if you click on the tag that follows it, you can see that that's the second part of that paragraph. Now, the fix for this is really quite simple, but I want to point out that the experience for the end user is going to be that it's going to read this text and it's going to stop abruptly after the [[Microsoft Word|word]], employment.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/dealing-with-content-that-spans-more-than-one-page?u=76281980&t=97)** So it's going to be a little bit of a strange experience because it just abruptly ends and then the user can read the next paragraph but then, of course, it's going to begin abruptly as well. So the way we can fix this is I'm going to up open up the tag for this text and I'm going to open up the tag for the paragraph that follows it. And I'm simply going to drag the content object, also called marked content, I'm going to drag it up and I'm going to drop it so that it all appears under that first paragraph. And with this paragraph now being empty, and we know it's empty because there's no little triangle to the left of it, we can right click on that and choose delete tag. You can also just hit the delete key on your keyboard. Now, to see this correctly, what I'm going to have you do is come up here to the View menu, go to Page Display, and choose Enable Scrolling. And the reason I did that is because now I can scroll my document to see the end of one page and the beginning of the other at the same time. And you could see that this content is now all inside of the same tag. And so from a [[User Experience (UX)|user experience]], it's not going to stop abruptly and it will now read as one continuous paragraph. Now, there's a couple more instances of this in this document and I'm going to have you go ahead and fix that.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/dealing-with-content-that-spans-more-than-one-page?u=76281980&t=188)** I'm going to do the same. And when we start with the next chapter, if you start with my file, all of them will be fixed at that point. So, as I said before, I wouldn't call this a failure condition, but it could create an odd experience for users of assistive technology. Taking the extra step to tag the content correctly will go a long way in improving the user experience for the end user.

#### [Tagging lists](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-lists?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-lists?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When bulleted or numbered lists appear in a document, they need to be tagged appropriately so that the user knows that the content is organized in a list. #### [Creating table of contents links](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/creating-table-of-contents-links?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/creating-table-of-contents-links?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Having a table of contents tagged correctly is a great start. But the point of a table of contents is to make it easier to get to that section of the document from the entry in the table of contents. You can see my cursor changes to a pointer, and when I click on that, it takes me to the page that that entry is linked to. It could be a bit of work, but creating links for your table of contents entry creates a better [[User Experience (UX)|user experience]] and provides an easy means for users of assistive technology to efficiently navigate and read content in a document.

#### [The Table Editor](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/the-table-editor?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/the-table-editor?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Tables typically require some massaging for full compliance. Some important things to remember about tables is that every table needs to have a header row. It can be a row header or a column header, but regardless it needs a header row to be compliant. Additionally, when cells are merged in a table, the appropriate span attribute needs to be applied to avoid the common regularity error that appears when checking your file. Tables are covered under WCAG success criteria 1.4.5 and 1.3.1. You'll also find them in the PDF techniques for WCAG in item number six. And in the PDF/UA specification, you'll find the description for tables in checkpoints nine and 15. The first thing I'm going to do just as a general check is in the accessibility tool, I'm going to go ahead and run my accessibility check. I'm just going to leave the document category chosen, and I'm going to choose start checking. And if you go under the table category here, you're going to find two main errors that are occurring. And one is that the headers are failing on the tables in my document. I can navigate to them to see which ones it is erroring on. And then I'm also getting a regularity error, and you're going to get that error on tables that have cells that have been merged. So I'm going to go ahead and close out of that and let's go ahead and open up our tags structure
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/the-table-editor?u=76281980&t=94)** because before I dive too deep into this, I want to make sure that my tables are set up for success. And one of the ways that we do that is if you go to the first table in your document, you want to make sure that visually, if a row has one cell, you want to make sure that that row only has one cell. And here you can see that the second row actually has three cells, right? There's a blank cell in that row, but there are only two cells in the structure. And that can cause a problem. A lot of people have a preconceived notion that empty cells in a table are bad. Fundamentally, they're not. I mean, if you have an empty cell, an empty cell is an empty cell. So in this case, what I need to do is I need to insert an empty cell to kind of occupy the space of that empty cell in that row. So what I'm going to do is I'm going to right click and I'm going to choose new tag and I'm going to choose table data cell to create a new empty cell. >
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/the-table-editor?u=76281980&t=191)** And what we're going to do is I'm just going to click on any one of these labels within this table to kind of make that the active table. >
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/the-table-editor?u=76281980&t=277)** And then I have one more table over on page 19. I'm just going to do the same thing for that one. Grab these three cells, right click, table cell properties, header cell for the column and I'm done. Notice that in the [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] of the table, there are three cells in every row. So to properly define this, health insurance needs to be told that it occupies the space of three. So to do that in the column span field, I'm going to change the value to three. And I'm going to go ahead and click OK. Now you're going to get a dialog box that to summarize is telling you that you can mess up your document if you're not careful. So for that reason,
>
> **[6:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/the-table-editor?u=76281980&t=369)** I highly encourage you before you dive in and start modifying your tables, do a save as. That way, you have a copy of your file and if you do by chance mess up your document, you can always go back to the good version and try again. I'm pretty confident that this is what I want to do. So I'm going to say yes. And then I'm going to go down to the dental insurance table, I'm going to click on the dental insurance cell, right click, table cell properties. This one only needs a column span of two because that cell only asked occupy the space of two cells. Click OK. Yes. I don't have to do anything for life insurance because it's basically a one column table, but retirement plan does need to be fixed. So I'm going to click on that cell, right click, table cell properties and set the column span to two. And if I now go back into my checker and I rerun my checker, you're going to see that under the tables category, everything is now passing. You can ignore the fact that the summary is skipped. For all recognized standards, the table summary is not required, and that's why the Acrobat checker skips it by default. Acrobat's table editor puts powerful controls in our hands to help us remediate tables appropriately. It's not perfect, but for most tables it does a fairly good job.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|Rest]] (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (6), find (4)

#### [Artifacting elements](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/artifacting-elements?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/artifacting-elements?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Artifacting is a term used to describe elements in a document that you do not want to be read by assistive technology. Common elements that require artifacting in a document are page headers and footers that appear on every page of a document, images that have no real meaning, and decorative elements that are strictly visual in nature and have no value. Artifacting objects streamlines the reading experience by only reading content that matters or has value. Let's dive into how to artifact objects in Adobe Acrobat. What I want to do is start with showing you how to not artifact objects in Acrobat. And I want to show you this because it's a common issue that we've been seeing. And the method that I want to show you not to do is found in your Reading Order panel. If you open up your Reading Order panel, you're going to notice that you have a button in the lower right-hand corner called Background/Artifact. And although that looks like a very obvious way to artifact objects, between you and me and everyone listening to this course, there is a bug that is inherent with using that button. And you're going to know that you've experienced the bug if you're tagging a document, and all of a sudden, it no longer allows you to tag content in the document using the Reading Order panel. And that typically happens immediately after you used the Background/Artifact button.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/artifacting-elements?u=76281980&t=95)** So for that reason, I encourage people not to use that method. So that being said, let's talk about what method you should use. So I'm going to go ahead and close this, and we're going to come over here and open up the Content pane. Now, the Content pane, as you can see here, is organized by page. You've got page 1 through 21 in our case. And if you open up the page 1, you're going to notice that as you look at this, you're going to kind of be able to see that everything on this page has a container. And that's a good thing. That means everything on the page has been tagged. Now, let's go to page 2. You can see in page 2, I have some objects here that are not inside of a container. So I have some paths here. If you scroll down, you're also going to notice, if you click on these, it'll highlight the object that is on the page. And so here is that running footer that I was talking about. So if you want to artifact an object, what you can do, so these paths here are the rectangles behind these shapes, and there's this line up here as well. So to artifact them, I can just click on the first one, Shift + click on the last one. And if you right-click, you can choose Create Artifact. Now, for general objects on the page, I typically encourage people to just make it a page artifact and go ahead and click OK. And then if you scroll down towards the bottom, you're going to see that we have
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/artifacting-elements?u=76281980&t=189)** our running footer information. So I'm going to select the path as well as the text, and I'm going to right-click and choose Create Artifact. Now, this is more of a recommendation, not really a mandatory thing, but the PDF/UA specification indicates that running headers and footers should be tagged as a pagination artifact. Now, between you and me, I've actually never seen any of the checkers fail a document for not artifacting it as a pagination artifact, but I just tend to do it as a best practice. So take that for what it's worth. Because this is in fact a running footer, I am going to make it a pagination artifact. And I'm going to attach it to the bottom because it is in fact at the bottom of the page. And then I'm going to go ahead and click OK. And you can see here that that footer is now artifacted, and so are those rectangles. Now, if you remember, when we were tagging the table of contents, I had instructed you that you can avoid tagging the dot leader. Now, even though they have containers in here, they're only tagged as a span, so it's not really relevant. And so what I'm going to do is I'm going to Control + click on all of those dot leaders. And I'm just going to select all of those leaders that are on this page. And again, this is probably something
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/artifacting-elements?u=76281980&t=282)** that is not going to cause a failure, but as a best practice, and to just kind of take all of the appropriate steps that I can, I'm going to select all of them, right-click, and choose Create Artifact. And again, I'll just make that a page artifact and click OK, and now you can see all of them have been converted over to an artifact. Let's do one more page. I'll go to page 3. On this page, we have some of these paths as well. So I'm going to select them, right-click, Create Artifact, click OK. If I want to, I can grab all of those dot leaders that are on this page. Again, this is more of a thorough artifacting job, but since I'm here, and since it doesn't take too long, I'm going to go ahead and artifact those. And then down here at the bottom of the page, I have the running footer for page 3. So I'm going to right-click, choose Create Artifact, make that a pagination artifact attached to the bottom, and click OK. I'm not going to have you watch me do the [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] of the pages. I'm going to let you go ahead and go through the rest of the document, artifact any of the unneeded objects, I'm going to do the same thing in the rest of the document, and if you want to start with the 03_11 document, it will automatically be artifacted. Now, one more thing to point out as we get up to page 1 here, you're going to notice that we do have a couple of images in here.
>
> **[6:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/artifacting-elements?u=76281980&t=377)** And this is another one of those cases where images are often artifacted as well. This is often a subjective decision. I would ask the question, what is the value of describing these bottles? And I would say in this case, it probably does in fact have value because I can describe the different scents or the flavors, I should say, of the different olive oils. But there may be cases, especially in a designed document, where imagery is used simply for aesthetic purposes. And you're going to want to ask yourself that question, is there any value in that image? And if the answer is no, you can go ahead and artifact it. I personally usually save the artifacting step for the end of the remediation process. Once all meaningful content is tagged, you can identify non-tagged content in the Content pane and artifact those items that have no value.

#### [Work with the Content pane](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/work-with-the-content-pane?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/work-with-the-content-pane?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] The content pane is a very powerful tool in Adobe Acrobat. But in my experience, it's very underutilized. In this video, I'd like to show you a few powerful ways that the content pane can come in handy when fixing issues in a PDF file. I've purposefully created a problem in this document so that I can show you how to fix it. Now, I'm going to go ahead and navigate to page two. And you'll notice in the main sections of this table of contents, the text has disappeared. And this is an issue that you can sometimes encounter in the tagging process, where you tag an item and all of a sudden the text disappears. And you're like, what just happened? And I could tell you what happened, during the tagging process, the stacking order of the objects changed. And because of that, a shape has been brought to the front, and is now covering up the text. So, if we open up the content pane and we go to page two, what you need to know about the content pane is that content in the content pane is content in your document. For example, if I click on this object, let's do this object here. If I were to right-click on this and choose delete, I'm literally going to delete that from the page. So you typically don't want to do that, but you need to understand that the content is really the content on the page.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/work-with-the-content-pane?u=76281980&t=93)** Now, in addition to the content in the content pane being the content on the page, it also controls the stacking order of objects in the document. So for example, if I click on this object and then I start tapping the down arrow key, you could see that it's highlighting the text behind that rectangle. And as I start arrowing down, it's just going to start highlighting the objects in the document. And so, what I'm going to do is I'm going to keep toggling down until it highlights those rectangles. And boom, there they are. So those rectangles are in this artifact object. And for some unbeknownst reason, they got moved to the front and are now covering up my text. Now, although the content pane does control the stacking order, the weird thing about it is the stacking order in the content pane is organized from bottom to top. What I mean by that is the bottom most objects in the content pane are front most in the stacking order. So to address this problem, I'm going to grab this object that is those rectangles, and I'm going to drag them up in the stacking order. I'm going to put them all the way up here at the top. And when I do, you're going to see that my text now reappears. So I'm hoping that gives you a little bit of insight into what is happening when this happens to you
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/work-with-the-content-pane?u=76281980&t=186)** when you're tagging your own documents. I highly encourage you not to drag anything in the order pane because rearranging content in the order pane can affect your tag structure. And being that we've been spending so much time getting that tag structure correct up to this point, I certainly don't want to mess it up now. That being said, by showing the order panel, I can see what's called the reading order in this document. Now this gets us into a whole other concept here, because there are in fact two different orders in a PDF file. We have the tag order, which is what most assistive software uses, including jaws and NVDA. And then you have the reading order, which is what we're looking at now. And some assistive technologies do, in fact, use the reading order instead of the tag order, which is why you always want both orders to be correct. And that is a weak hag requirement, it's actually talked about in success criteria 1.3.2. So if you want to look that up, you can read more about that. Now that being said, to fix the order here, because things are reading slightly out of order. So what I'm going to do
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/work-with-the-content-pane?u=76281980&t=278)** is I'm actually going to go to my content pane, but I'm also going to open up my reading order panel so that I can see the reading order of the objects as I'm making adjustments. And you can see here, if you look at the content pane, I'm going to scroll all the way to the bottom, which is where I am going to find. Oops, that's the wrong page here. Let's go to page one. You could see up here at the top this is the figure number one. And you can see it is in the first position. So if I want to fix the reading order, what I need to do is drag this, and I'm going to move it below the next item. And when I do, you're going to see that my reading order is now reading in the correct order. So the reading order does follow your content order. However, it does go top to bottom. And I know this gets confusing guys. So I'm trying to give you this information in digestible chunks. But what I typically do in this case is I will just navigate from page to page, making sure that the reading order on my pages is in fact reading in the correct order. This particular document is a very linear document. So I don't really see many issues here, but with a more designed document and a more, I'll call it a bitsy document, if you will, where you just have chunks of information all over the page,
>
> **[6:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/work-with-the-content-pane?u=76281980&t=373)** your order is not quite as automatic as we're seeing in this document. But I think you're seeing here that there's more than meets the eye to the content pane. I would encourage you to make use of it to fix your stacking order issues, reading order issues, as well as content issues.

#### [Add alternative text](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-alternative-text-14413940?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-alternative-text-14413940?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When a PDF document is read by assistive technology, images and graphics inherently have no value. Images and graphics can't be read because they typically don't contain any real content. To overcome this limitation, we need to add alternative text to those graphics to describe their purpose. Alternative text is covered in the WCAG success criteria 1.1.1 and the PDF techniques for WCAG in item number one, and in PDF/UA checkpoint 13. One of the things you're going to notice if you run the Accessibility Check in Acrobat is that we're going to get some errors here for alternate text. And there are a number of figures in my document that contain no alternate text. So we need to fix that problem. To do that, we can accomplish it a couple of different ways. One way is using the reading order panel. So I'm going to go ahead and go to page one and I'm going to open up my reading order panel. And if I click on the first figure, I'm going to click on this little label right here, and then I'm going to right click and I'm going to choose Edit Alternate Text. And in a case of a logo like this, I'm really just trying to convey exactly what the logo says. So I'm simply going to type in here Two Trees Olive Oil. In alternate text, I try to avoid using punctuation
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-alternative-text-14413940?u=76281980&t=95)** whenever possible because in an alternate text field, the punctuation is red. So in the case of this, it's one line I'm going to alleviate or eliminate the period. So I'm going to click Okay, then I'll go ahead and go down to figure two and I'll right click and choose edit alternate text. And now here, the general premise of alternate text is to describe the image in its context. And you want to describe it as concisely as possible while still conveying the appropriate information. So for this alternate text, I'm going to type in here "An array of two trees olive oil showing rosemary, lemon, garlic, basil, and chili flavors". And then I'll go ahead and click Okay. Now, another way you can add alternate text is in the Tags pane. So what I'm going to do is I'm going to go into my Tags pane and I'm going to find the next figure. I'm going to click on it and I can now right click on this and choose properties. And right here is the alternate text field. So in this case, I might type something like "A female employee at the Two Trees processing facility
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-alternative-text-14413940?u=76281980&t=185)** sifting through olives for defects". I would like to tell people I'm a designer and I'm the worst person in the world to write alternate text for figures, but there are times when I'm remediating documents, I will often ask the client for alternate text, but I typically don't get it. So I do the best job that I can. To be fair, I really feel like this task is best suited for somebody in an editorial role, somebody whose job is to author and edit content. And then I'll just go down to think I have two more figures in here that I need to find. Here's another one, right? I'm going to go ahead and close this and now I'll save it. And that inherently is the requirement. Again, I'm going to use the Acrobat checker mainly because it's convenient right now. And you'll see that all of my alternate text requirements are now passing.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-alternative-text-14413940?u=76281980&t=277)** That's the key is to make sure that you've added alternate text to all imagery in a document so that it has a description. Adding this alternate text to graphics just ensures that users of assistive technology can understand the meaning of those graphics and can interpret the information in the document appropriately.

#### [Add bookmarks](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-bookmarks?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-bookmarks?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Tutor] WCAG requires that bookmarks are included for any documents that contain more than nine pages. So for those documents, you should add bookmarks to assist users in navigating those documents. I prefer to add bookmarks to a document towards the end of the process for reasons I'm about to show you. Bookmarks are described in WCAG success criteria, two dot four dot five, as well as PDF techniques for WCAG in PDF item number two. I'm going to go ahead and come up here and open my bookmarks panel simply to show you that currently there are no bookmarks in my document. Now, to add bookmarks to this document, the way that I approach this is, I make sure that I've already tagged the content in the document, the way that we've been learning throughout this chapter. And the reason I say that is because, Acrobat makes it really easy to add bookmarks once you've tagged the document. The way that we can do that is by coming up here to the options button in the bookmarks panel and I'm going to choose new bookmarks from structure. And when it says structure, it's referring to your tag structure and you'll notice in the structure elements dialog box, it's literally showing you every tag that appears in your document. Now, bookmarks logically are typically going to be referencing all of the headings in your document.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-bookmarks?u=76281980&t=94)** So I'm going to come up here and click on H one and then I'm going to shift click on H two to select both of those heading structure elements. And when I click okay, you're going to see a new category has been created and I'm literally going to rename this to simply bookmarks. But now when I open this up, you're going to see that virtually all of my bookmarks have automatically been created. If I want to know about paydays, I click on that bookmark and it takes me to that section of the document. If I want to know about vacation, I click on that and it'll take me to that section of the document. Now, sometimes it can be helpful to organize these bookmarks hierarchically. And what I mean by that is, for example, under section one, I really only have one item here, but what you could do is if you drag up into the right, you can nest that inside of section one. And if I want to, I can grab all the items for section two and nest that under section two and so on and so forth. It's just a good way to further organize your bookmarks so that they're a little bit more easy to access and you can collapse these when they're not being used and organize them in a really nice way. So it's certainly not a requirement, but it can certainly make it a little bit easier for users to navigate the content in your document.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-bookmarks?u=76281980&t=190)** So I'm going to go ahead and collapse these and now you can see that my bookmarks are organized the way that I've configured them. Again, after you've appropriately tagged the document, your bookmarks can be easily created in Adobe Acrobat by creating the bookmarks from structure and that easily meets the WCAG requirement for success criteria, two dot four dot five.

#### [Tagging footnotes](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-footnotes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-footnotes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Footnotes contain references as well as the footnotes themselves, both of which need to be tagged appropriately. We can find the tags needed in the Reading Order panel, which is very convenient. And footnotes are covered in WCAG success criteria 1.3.1, as well as the PDF/UA checkpoint number 19. I'm going to go ahead and navigate to the last page of the document, where at the bottom of the page, you're going to see a footnote that has been used on this page. Now, I'm going to go ahead and [[Zoom]] in a little bit more just to make it easier for us to see what we're doing here. And you'll also notice that right here is a footnote reference. Now, I'm going to go ahead and open my Reading Order panel. And the first thing we need to do is we need to tag that number. So using my cursor, I'm going to click and drag so that I only have that number selected. And over here in my Reading Order panel, I can click on the Reference button, or I can just tap the letter Q on my keyboard to automatically tag that number as a reference. And then at the bottom of the page, I can click and drag to select the footnote itself. And I can then tag that as a note, or I can use the Z key on my keyboard to automatically tag that as a note. Now, once that's done, I can go ahead and close the Reading Order panel.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-footnotes?u=76281980&t=94)** But what I now need to do is go into my tag structure to fine tune how those items are read. So I'm going to go ahead and click on the paragraph, and you'll see that when I click on this paragraph, and your results may be slightly different depending on how you tagged it, but I'm going to open up this P tag. And if you look in here, you'll notice that the text begins, and then there's a reference tag for the number, and then the text continues from there. Now, what you're also going to notice, and I want to be quite honest, I think I lucked out here because the note tag does not always know where in the tag structure it's supposed to go. Now, that being said, the traditional approach here is to position the note as the last item in the paragraph where the reference has been created. And the idea there is that as the reader is reading the content, they'll read the reference for number one. And then immediately when they're finished reading the paragraph, they can read the footnote itself. You'll notice there are no links in this footnote. And so where, with an endnote, it's a similar situation, but typically with endnotes, the reference is a link to the endnote in the back of the book. But with a footnote, because all the content is on the same page, the generally accepted practice
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-footnotes?u=76281980&t=188)** is to have the footnote read at the very end of the paragraph in which the reference occurs. So I'm going to go ahead and close out of that, and I think now that you've tagged your footnote appropriately, your document is well on its way to full compliance.
