#### [Walking the tags tree](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/walking-the-tags-tree?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/walking-the-tags-tree?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] As useful and helpful as automated checkers are, they are far from perfect. For this reason, there's a step in the remediation process that only we as humans can perform. That step is called walking the tags tree. This step basically consists of a human walking through the tags tree to ensure that objects are A, tagged appropriately and B, are being read in the correct order. A checker can tell you if a figure is missing alternate text, but it doesn't know if texts should be a heading or if elements are out of order. Let me show you how to walk the tree in this document. I'm going to go ahead and come over here and open up my tags paint. Now, one of the things that I want to point out, and this is an issue that the Acrobat checker is not going to complain about, but it's one that other checkers may complain about. And for that reason, I always encourage people to do one more step before we continue here. And that is the fact that currently all of my tags are at the root level of the tag structure. And what that means is that they're first in line, when we're looking at our tag structure and what that does is it kind of makes it hard to establish hierarchy. So for that reason, what I like to do and what's encouraged is that all of the tags in our document are contained inside of a document tag. So to do that, what I'm going to do is I'm going to click on this tags icon here, and I'm going to right click and choose new tag.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/walking-the-tags-tree?u=76281980&t=97)** And from the list I'm going to choose document, and then I'm going to click, okay. Now what happens is that document tag goes all the way to the bottom. So what I'm going to do is I'm going to click on the first tag in my list. Then I'm going to shift click on the last tag to select all the tags in my document. And then I'm just going to drag them and nest them inside of the document tag. And when that's done, you'll notice now that the document tag is at the root element, basically the first level under the tags tree. And then all of the other tags are children of the document tag, which is really, really important. Now to walk the tags tree, it's really quite simple. I'm going to click on the first tag in my list of items. And what I'm going to do is I'm going to tap the down arrow key on my keyboard to see the order in which content is being read. And I'm noticing first off that the logo is being read second. And the image in the middle of the page is being read first. So I'm simply going to drag them to rearrange them so that now the logo is going to be read, then the figure, then employee manual. And I'm just going to keep tapping the down arrow key and just kind of review to make sure that all of my content is being read in the appropriate order. And I'm not going to have you guys watch me as I go through
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/walking-the-tags-tree?u=76281980&t=190)** every page, but I do want to show you a couple of things that can be really helpful. So again, I'm just checking the order right now. Now one of the things that's also helpful, let me just scroll up so you can see this. In addition to the up and down arrow keys on your keyboard to kind of go from one tag to the other. You can also tap the right arrow key on your keyboard to expand a tag, to see the contents within. And now I can go to the down arrow key and hit the right arrow again and I can open up these tags to look at their content. Conversely, the left arrow key allows me to collapse tags back to their collapsed state. So walking the tags tree is simply a process of making sure that as I highlight an object, I can see that this is an H2. That's what it should be. That is a list. And I can see that the order in which the content is being read is also correct. And I would continue walking this tags tree through the end of the document. And you could go pretty fast too. As you go through here, I mean, I'm really just looking to make sure that everything is being read in the order that I intend it to be read. And when I get to the very end, I have basically completed the process. So that is the process of simply walking the tags tree to ensure that my content is not only tagged correctly, but being read in the correct order. And as you can see this is a crucial step,
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/walking-the-tags-tree?u=76281980&t=284)** it's not very hard, but it's important. And it's an important step to ensure that the experience for a user of assistive technology is equal to the experience for any other user.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

#### [Checking color contrast](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/checking-color-contrast?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/checking-color-contrast?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Ensuring the text elements in your document have sufficient contrast is a key requirement for ensuring that low vision users are able to read text in the document. Let's take a look at a few methods we can use to check color contrast. Now, one of the things that I want to point out here, as we are working on a PDF file, is that checking the color contrast at this point in the process is really too late because the cake is already baked, so to speak. The PDF file has already been generated and if, at this point we do determine that we do not have sufficient contrast, it's really hard to fix at this point. So my advice to you is check the color contrast as early on in the process as possible. You really want to be doing this as you're building the document in the source application. Now, a tool that I'd like to reference you to is one that's called the Color Contrast Analyzer, and this is made by TPGi and it's a free utility that you can download and utilize to check the color contrast between the text and its background. You can see here, you can define a foreground color and a background color and down at the bottom, it'll tell you whether that color contrast passes at various levels of the WCAG standard. So it's really, really valuable and it's a great tool to use in your source application
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/checking-color-contrast?u=76281980&t=94)** to make sure that your contrast is sufficient. Now to verify that the color contrast is sufficient in my PDF, I can certainly use the Color Contrast Analyzer, but a tool that I've grown to appreciate is the PAC 2021 Checker. And what I love about the PAC 2021 Checker is that I can run a file through this checker and if I look at my results in detail and go down to the WCAG category under Perceivable and Distinguishable, you can see that the PAC 2021 Checker checks the color contrast of the text in the document. So even if you have gone through and verified that your contrast is sufficient, this is a great double check to just validate that yes, in fact, the color contrast is sufficient. I have found that the PAC 2021 Checker occasionally will give me kind of a false error because it has trouble identifying the elements on the page. But for the most part, it does a really good job. So those are some of the tools that I like to use for checking color contrast, and ensuring that your text elements have color contrast between the text and the background of 4.5 to one contrast ratio will ensure that all users can easily read that content in your document.

### 4. Make an Existing PDF File Accessible (New Interface)

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Add metadata](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-metadata-25575372?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-metadata-25575372?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There are certain [[Metadata]] required in a PDF file in order to make it accessible. Specifically, a document title is required and that title is required to be displayed when a PDF file is open. Although the document title is the only required metadata element in a PDF file, due to the fact that most PDF files will be posted online, it can be really helpful to add other metadata to the file to assist in [[Search Engine Optimization (SEO)|search engine optimization]]. Or basically helping people find the document when they're searching for it online. The title requirement for a PDF file can be found in the WCAG specifications under success criteria 2.4.2 page titled. So I'm starting here with my 0401 document open on my computer. And to add metadata to this document in the new Acrobat interface, we're going to come up here to the menu button and then we're going to go way down here to where it says document properties. #### [Tag content with the Reading Order tool](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tag-content-with-the-reading-order-tool-25580385?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tag-content-with-the-reading-order-tool-25580385?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Fundamentally, tags are how we identify the value of content in a document so that it can be read and interpreted using assistive technology. By applying a tag to an object, we're basically assigning semantic value to the object. For example, a P tag is used to identify body text in a document. Where an H1, H2, or H3 tag, and so forth are used to identify heading tags in a document. Figure tag identifies figures, tables identifies tables, and list tag identifies lists. So identifying heading tags is critical in helping users of assistive technology to interpret the information in a document, and is covered by WCAG Success Criterion 1.3.1, Info and Relationships. So what I'm going to do here is I'm going to show you how we can tag a document. Now, the first thing I want to do to show you this is I'm going to open up my tags pane over here on the right. And you can see that when I open the tags pane, currently it tells me that there are no tags available. And honestly, this is kind of worst case scenario, right? Because in a situation like this, even though many assistive technologies have the ability to kind of auto-tag a document so you can read it, the value of that or how well it does is certainly questionable. Nothing beats a well tagged, well organized document.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tag-content-with-the-reading-order-tool-25580385?u=76281980&t=96)** So to do this, we are going to leverage our accessibility tools inside of Adobe Acrobat. So we're going to come up here to the upper left where it says all tools. I'm going to choose prepare for accessibility. And then I'm going to click on the fix reading order option. #### [Dealing with content that spans more than one page](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/dealing-with-content-that-spans-more-than-one-page-25577340?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/dealing-with-content-that-spans-more-than-one-page-25577340?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] It's pretty normal to have textual content that flows from one page to another in a document. However, when tagging content using Adobe Acrobat, it's not possible to select content on more than one page at a time. Furthermore, some applications are better at dealing with these situations than other applications, and may often split content across a page creating a less-than-desirable [[User Experience (UX)|user experience]]. Let's take a look at how to deal with this situation. Now, I'm working in the 0404.pdf file, and I'm simply going to start navigating through the pages to find a situation that I want to address. So here on page five, we can see that the text at the bottom of the page kind of stops mid-sentence and then continues on the next page. Now it's one thing if the content is tagged properly, but it's another if it's tagged incorrectly. So to figure out where we are in this document, I'm going to go ahead and open my Tags pane. And I'm just going to select some text in the paragraph in question. And I'm going to come up here to my three-dot menu, and I'm going to choose Find Tag from Selection. And what that'll do, is it'll highlight the tag, here we go, that's being worked on. So if I click on this P tag, we can see that it highlights that entire area. And what I'm going to do, also, is I'm going to change how my pages are displayed.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/dealing-with-content-that-spans-more-than-one-page-25577340?u=76281980&t=95)** It'll make it a little bit easier for me to see this. So I'm going to come up here to my Menu button, and I'm going to go down to the View sub menu, and I'm going to go to Page display, and I'm going to turn on Enable scrolling. And what that's going to do, is that now, I can kind of scroll halfway across a page and kind of see the end of one page, beginning of another. And you could see here, that even though technically this is one big paragraph, with the exception of the footer, ignore that, but, you know, this content and this content is really one single paragraph, but if we look at how this is tagged, it is tagged as two separate paragraphs. Now, what's the problem with that? Well, from a user experience standpoint, when a user is reading this content, it's going to read this information and then it's going to stop, and it's going to say, "Okay, what do you want me to do next?" And I can of course, continue reading if I want to, but then it's going to start, basically, midway through the sentence. And so it's fine if you're reading them consecutively, but it is a bit awkward because it's going to pause and not read it as one single paragraph. So the fix for this is fairly simple. I will tell you that Adobe InDesign is particularly good about handling this. You really will not see this situation when exporting out of InDesign,
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/dealing-with-content-that-spans-more-than-one-page-25577340?u=76281980&t=188)** but other applications such as [[Microsoft Word]], and I think maybe [[Microsoft PowerPoint|PowerPoint]], tends to break the content mid-sentence when it goes from one page to another. So the way that we can fix this is I'm simply going to open the P tag to see the content within it, and then I'm going to open the P tag of the following paragraph, and I'm simply going to grab these content objects and I'm going to move them up into the first P tag. And that's going to leave this P tag empty, which means I can delete it. So, I'm going to right-click and say Delete Tag. And now when I highlight this paragraph, you'll notice that it's highlighting the first and second part of the paragraph is one unit. And now when that's read using assistive technology, it's going to voice it appropriately. And so we have a couple other examples of this. As we get down to this section here, I'm just going to find to Tag from Selection, there it is. And again, you can see that the adjacent paragraph has a couple of lines. And again, I would just merge these into one and then delete that empty P tag, and now it'll be tagged properly as a single paragraph. Now I want to show you another example where it's probably even more of a bad experience. Here you can see... What I'm going to do is,
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/dealing-with-content-that-spans-more-than-one-page-25577340?u=76281980&t=280)** I'm just going to find the Tag from Selection, there we go, and what you'll notice here is that in this situation, this list is broken into two distinct and separate lists. And so what that's going to do from a user standpoint, is when we get to this list, it's going to say list with five items, and it's going to read all of the items and then it's going to stop. And then they're going to keep going and it's going to say list with five more items. And then it's going to read the items in that list. So it's certainly going to be awkward, 'cause you're like, "Well, why are there two separate lists? "Why are they separated into two separate objects?" And once again, the solution is fairly simple. You open up this list tag, for the first group of items, you can see that I have five list items went in there. And if I open the second list, I've got five more items. So the solution here is to simply select the list items from the second list, move them into the first list, and now I can delete that empty list. And now you can see that this list is tagged as one item. And now when the user is reading this, it's going to say list with 10 items. And then they can navigate through that list, and they can read each item in the list in a very friendly way, for lack of a better term. So again, when you run into this situation, and you will, depending on the applications you use,
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/dealing-with-content-that-spans-more-than-one-page-25577340?u=76281980&t=376)** depending on how you tag the document, I believe even autotag will break these lists up across a page like this. So, you're going to have to fix that problem when it occurs. So, I wouldn't call this situation a failure condition per se, but it could create an odd experience for users who are using assistive technology. Taking the extra step to tag the content correctly will go a long way in improving the experience for the end user.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[User Experience (UX)|User experience]] (2), [[Microsoft Word]] (1), [[Microsoft PowerPoint|Powerpoint]] (1)
> **UI Navigation:** open the (3), click on (1), go to (1), right-click (1), select the (1)
> **CLI Commands:** find (4), make (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (1)
> **Speakers:** - [narrator] (1)

#### [Tagging lists](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-lists-25578357?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-lists-25578357?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When bulleted or numbered lists appear in a document, they need to be tagged appropriately so that the user knows that the content is organized in a list. As a cited user, the list is known by the bullet character or number that proceeds each item. For a user of assistive technology, that same meaning needs to be explained. This requirement is covered in WCAG, as well as in the PDF U/A standards. In WCAG, lists are covered in success criteria 1.3.1 Info and Relationships. Info and relationships essentially just says tag content as what it is. And in the case of a list, if the objects are lists, in fact, then they need to be tagged as such. In PDF Techniques for WCAG, PDF step 21 talks about using list tags for lists in PDF documents. And in PDF UA Standard, checkpoint 16 talks about lists as well. So here I am in the 04_05 document, and I'm going to navigate in my document to an area where I have some lists. And the first one that you'll find is in Section Three: Employment Policies. Now I'm going to open up my tags pane, and I'm going to kind of show you something here. And of course, I intentionally did this for the sake of explaining how to properly tag lists in Adobe Acrobat. But if you highlight the first item or any of the items in this list, and you come over here to the tags pane
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-lists-25578357?u=76281980&t=94)** and click on the three dots and choose find tag from selection, you'll receive an error message that says the selection was not found. And that essentially is telling us that this list is not tagged. So it certainly needs to be, but currently it is not. Now what I might do is highlight some of the text in the paragraph above and find that, and that will show me where that item is located. And that's located right here. So I'm going to [[Zoom]] in a little bit to make this process a little bit easier to see and to kind of see what I'm doing here. And I'm going to come up here to where it says all tools. I'm going to choose prepare for accessibility, and then I'm going to choose fixed reading order. And as we've done in the past, this opens up our reading order panel. Now that they're tagged, I can close the reading order panel and I'm going to come over here into my tags pane. So what I'm going to do is I'm going to click on the first P tag, and then I'm going to hold down my control key,
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-lists-25578357?u=76281980&t=285)** and I'm essentially going to click on every other P tag. And if you look at what you're selecting on the page, it's basically going to be all of the bullet characters. Now again, the tag for these bullet characters should not be a P tag. And in the type dropdown menu at the top of this dialogue, I'm going to scroll to where it says label. And that's the proper tag that you want assigned to the bullet character or the number in the case of a numbered list. And the type at the top here, this is going to change to, this is called list item body. So within each item of the list, you're going to have a label and an L body, and that's the proper structure for a list. Now, the way that lists themselves are structured, you have a list tag, and then each item of the list needs to be in a list item tag.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-lists-25578357?u=76281980&t=380)** Now what I'm going to have to do here, there are seven items in this list. > **[7:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-lists-25578357?u=76281980&t=474)** and I can nest them inside of the list. And then within each list item, we have a label and an L body. So that's a label and a list item body. So that is how lists need to be properly structured. Now in this document, I left you with a couple of other lists that need to be properly tagged, and I'm going to basically give that to you as homework, if you will. So I encourage you to continue on with this document and give yourself a little bit of practice by tagging those other lists the way that we did this one. So as you can see, tagging lists appropriately will ensure that they're read correctly using assistive technology, and that the reading experience is appropriate for the end user.

#### [Tagging your table of contents](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-table-of-contents-25576412?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-table-of-contents-25576412?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Documents that contain a table of contents should have those elements tagged appropriately. The PDF format provides a TOC tag that is used to identify a table of contents when it appears in a document. The use of the TOC tag is defined in WCAG success criteria 1.3.1, info and relationships. The reality is, if you tag those dot leaders, they will in fact be read. Now, there was a time when JAWS and NVDA would've read every one of those dots, but both of them now have implemented

So both of those programs have realized reading every single dot is not beneficial to anybody and so it'll just say changes in policy, dot, dot, dot. Now again, is the dot, dot, dot helpful? So I could come in here and say, "I don't want that, don't tag it." And now I can hit the text paragraph button and I'm just going to keep going down here, tagging these areas that I want to be tagged. And again, I got a little too much there. #### [Creating table of contents links](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/creating-table-of-contents-links-25576414?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/creating-table-of-contents-links-25576414?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Lecturer] Having the table of contents correctly tagged is a great start. But the point of a table of contents is to make it easier to get to that section of the document from the entry within that table of contents. When starting the PDF remediation process from a source document, this can happen automatically. For example, Adobe InDesign and [[Microsoft Word]] can automatically give you a linked table of contents, but tagging A PDF from scratch requires us to do the work ourselves. To get started, I'm going to create a destination to where I want the entry in my table of contents to go, and then I'm going to link to that destination. So I'm going to navigate to my table of contents in this document. And you can see here that the beginning of the table of contents I'm linking to Section 1: Introduction and 1.1 Changes In Policy, both of which are on page three. So I'm going to go ahead and navigate to that page. And over here in my navigation pane on the right, I'm going to right click and I'm going to choose destinations, and that's going to open up my destination's pane. Now what I'm going to do is with my cursor, I'm going to highlight this text that says Section 1: Introduction. And then at the top of the destination's pane, I'm going to click on the create new destination button. And that's going to create a destination and that is going to give me the ability to give this destination a name. Now what I'm going to do is I'm going to call this
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/creating-table-of-contents-links-25576414?u=76281980&t=94)** 01_Section 1: Introduction. Now I'm doing this on purpose. When you're linking to destinations, if you just name them based on the name they can appear out of order. So by putting a numerical number in front of it, it makes the process a bit easier. #### [Tagging tables](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-tables-25576413?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-tables-25576413?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Tables are an extremely useful element in a document as it allows all users to interpret the information within that table. Tables are a good way of conveying a lot of information to the reader in a limited amount of space. In order for tables to be read correctly using assistive software, the table needs to be tagged correctly. Let me walk you through the steps to appropriately tag a table in Adobe Acrobat. So I'm working here with the 0408 document already open, and I'm going to navigate to page 17 in this document where we have some tables. Now I'd like to start by kind of evaluating this table. And one of the rabbit holes that people often will find themselves in, is they'll see a table such as this and they'll think that they have to tag it as one big table. Doing so makes the process quite a bit more complicated. And to be honest, to do that in Acrobat would be quite difficult. So rather than tagging this as one big table, I would encourage you to tag these as four separate tables. We would tag this as one. We would tag this guy as another one. This one would be a table. And then of course, this one down here would be another table. And that's really going to simplify things for us. Now, just like we did in previous videos, to tag this table, we're going to start by opening up our reading order panel. So I'm going to come up here to the all tools button.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-tables-25576413?u=76281980&t=95)** I'm going to choose prepare for accessibility, and then I'm going to click on fixed reading order. And using my cursor, you can select various areas of this table. Now, one of the things you'll notice if I marque this entire table right here, there is a button in the reading order panel called Table, and if you click that button, it's going to seem as if it tagged that table correctly. However, let's take a closer look at that table. So I'm going to find that table in this document. Let me highlight some text and say find tag from selection. There we go. And what you're going to notice is that overall it looks pretty good, but you could see here, even though this should be three separate cells, for whatever reason, the reading order panel tagged it as one cell. And if I go to the next row, that one is tagged as three cells. But as you can see, it's missing some of the data and it actually put those two in a separate row. So my point of showing you that is that as much as I'd like to tell you that you can just click on the table button and it'll magically tag your table correctly, the reality is it's probably going to generate more work for you than tagging the table manually. So what I'm going to do here is I'm going to go ahead and close this document without saving it,
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-tables-25576413?u=76281980&t=192)** and then I'm going to reopen that file. So I'm going to go to prepare for accessibility, fixed reading order. And instead of tagging this as a table using a single button, what we can do is tag the individual parts of it as a cell. So I'm going to [[Zoom]] in a little bit here, to make this a little bit easier for everybody to see. Both of those are going to go into a cell, and then we'll do this guy, this guy, and then finally the last one right here. Now our job is to structure that content into a table. So if we go to our tags pain and we scroll down a little bit, we're going to find a bunch of TD cells. And these are all the cells that we tagged. So the way the tables are structured, the smallest unit within a table is the cell. And a cell can be either a TD for table data or a TH for table header. And then those cells are organized into rows. So this particular table has one, two, three, four, five rows. So in my tags pane, I'm going to click on the tag above the first TD tag. >
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-tables-25576413?u=76281980&t=352)** until I have a total of five rows. They are, I always say blank cells are okay for a sighted user. They're also okay for users of assistive technology. So what I need to do here is I need to actually create a blank cell to occupy that space. So I'm just going to right click and say new tag, and I'm going to choose table data cell. And you could see, this cell compared to this one. See how this has a triangle, which means it has content, this one doesn't because it's empty. So I'm just going to grab these three cells, move them into the second row, then I'll grab the next three cells, move them into the third row, grab the next three cells, move them into the fourth row, and then the last three cells will go into the fifth row. And that looks pretty good. So now we have our content organized by row. And then the last step in the process is to organize all of those table row tags into a table tag. So again, I'm going to click on the P tag above the first TR, right click new tag, and we're going to choose table. And then we'll grab all five of the rows
>
> **[7:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-tables-25576413?u=76281980&t=447)** and we're going to nest them as children inside of that table. And now you can see that our table is tagged appropriately. Now the other thing we need to do in this table is we're going to have to structure the header rows of this table. Now we're going to tackle that in the next video where we're going to dive into the table editor. But for now, understand that this is how a table needs to be structured. And I encourage you all to go ahead and use what you've just learned to tag some of the other tables in this document as well.

#### [The Table Editor](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/the-table-editor-25576415?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/the-table-editor-25576415?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Tables typically require some massaging for full compliance. Some important things to remember about tables is that every table needs to have a header row. It could be a row header or a column header, but regardless, it needs a header row. Additionally, when cells are merged in a table, the appropriate span attribute needs to be applied to avoid the common regularity error that appears when checking your file Tables are covered under WCAG Success Criteria 1.3.1, Info and Relationships. So I'm working in the 04_09 document here, and I'm going to open up my Tags pane, and I'm going to scroll down to where I have some tables in this document. One is that the headers for these tables is failing. Okay, that's one issue. And the other error is something called a regularity error. Now, a regularity error occurs when each row of a table does not have the same number of cells.

So after telling this cell to be a header cell, I'm going to change the scope to column so that it knows that it's a header for the column of the table. I'm going to go ahead and click OK. And so what I'm going to do is I'm going to click back on this health insurance cell, and basically what we want to do is we want to tell this cell that it occupies the space of three cells. And if you're curious about the table summary, there was a time several years ago where table summaries or basically a description of the table was required. That is no longer required by the WCAG standard. So Acrobat automatically skips the summary because it's not required. Once again, you can right click on that and you can choose to turn off the skipping of the rule, but then it's going to show up as an error because it failed. So basically we just skip the summary. It's no longer required. And that then in turn addresses all of our table issues. So Acrobat's Table Editor puts powerful control in our hands to help us remediate tables appropriately. It's not perfect, but for most tables, it does a fairly good job.

#### [Artifacting elements](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/artifacting-elements-25577341?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/artifacting-elements-25577341?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Artifacting is a term used to describe elements in a document that you do not want to be read by assistive technology. Common elements that require artifacting are page headers and footers that appear at the top and bottom of every page, images that have no real meaning, and decorative elements that are strictly visual in nature and have no value. Artifacting objects streamlines the reading experience by only reading content that matters or has value. Let's dive into how to artifact objects in Adobe Acrobat. Now, before I go too far, I want to show you one of the features in Adobe Acrobat that I would recommend you do not use because it causes problems when you're artifacting content. And that method is found if you come over here to the All tools button, go to Prepare for Accessibility and then choose Fix reading order. Now, in the Reading Order pane, you have a lot of buttons in here that can be used when you're tagging content. And one of the buttons that you're going to notice is called Background/Artifact. And if I highlight one of these objects, you'll see that the Background/Artifact button becomes available. Now, although it would seem that this would be the appropriate way to artifact content, there is in fact a bug in Acrobat that when you use this button, at some point Acrobat will stop allowing you to tag any of the content in your document.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/artifacting-elements-25577341?u=76281980&t=93)** And it becomes really frustrating when you're trying to get work done and all of a sudden you can no longer tag anything in your file. So that's why I recommend you do not use this option. It is absolutely a bug, I've run into it many times, and I simply encourage you not to use this method. Instead, I want to show you a method that you can use that will work. So I'm going to go ahead and close this, and I'm going to come over here to my Content pane. Now, I love using the Content pane to evaluate elements in my document because it makes it really easy to see items in your document that currently are not tagged. So if I open up my document here and I open up Page 1, Page 1 doesn't really have anything that's not tagged, everything on the page has a tag, it's appropriate, it looks good. But if we go to Page 2, you're going to see that we've got several objects up here at the top that are not in a container, and that effectively means that they're not tagged. Now, if you click on one of those objects, you can see that the first path is the rule at the top of the page. And then the other paths are the rectangles that are being used as the visual background for those text elements. Now, the PDF/UA standard requires everything in a document to have a tag.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/artifacting-elements-25577341?u=76281980&t=190)** The tag can be a header, it can be a figure, it could be a table, it could be whatever you want, but it has to have a tag. And when content is not tagged, it produces an error when you go to validate your file. So the Content pane is a great place that you can go in order to artifact content. So all of these paths appear at the top of the page, we do not need them to be read and we should artifact them. And the way that you do that is with these paths selected, we're simply going to right click and we're going to choose Create Artifact. And that's going to bring up the Create Artifact dialogue box. You don't really have to choose anything specific in here. Typically I leave the Artifact Type to a Page and then I don't choose any attachment to the page. It's really not necessary. And so when you click OK, you're going to notice that all of those paths are now placed inside of an artifact container, and that is basically how content is properly artifacted. You will see the artifact in the Content pane, you never see the artifact in the Tags pane. So that's why the Content pane is a little bit more helpful 'cause you could go through your document, you could see elements that are artifacted or that need to be artifacted. The Content pane's also helpful too, like here, I'm going to click on this little object that says Text. And it appears that that's just a space that exists after the letter Y
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/artifacting-elements-25577341?u=76281980&t=284)** that didn't get tagged when I was tagging the content. In a case like that, you can literally right click on that object and just choose Delete. You can delete it from the page 'cause we don't need it. I would not encourage you to do that with any other object because when you delete content from the Content pane, you delete it from the page. Content is content, so you don't want to really delete anything of value in the Content pane. And then at the bottom of the page, here we have the path as well as the running footer. I'm going to right click on those and choose Create Artifact. And when I click OK, those two elements are now nested inside of an artifact container, and now those items are properly artifacted. Now the Content pane isn't the only place where you can go to artifact your content. You can also artifact content in the Tags Pane. And I'm going to come up here to the top of my Tags tree, and the second figure tag from the top is this image of the bottles of olive oil. Now, the document that we're working on is an employee manual. And the question arises, how valuable is a photo of a couple bottles of olive oil in the context of the employee manual? I mean, the first figure up here is the Two Trees Olive Oil company logo. And that does make sense to have in our document because it is the employee manual.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/artifacting-elements-25577341?u=76281980&t=380)** But the bottles of olive oil, they're a little bit questionable. Now, I'm sure if I surveyed 10 people and said, "Should I keep this image or shouldn't I?" Half of them will say yes and half of them will say no. So it's important to understand that this is a subjective decision, but you want to have some intelligence behind that decision because you want to ask yourself, "If a user is reading this document using assistive technology, how valuable is it that they know that there's a photo of five bottles of olive oil on the front cover of the employee manual?" And so in my opinion, the answer would be not very valuable. So, in the Tags pane, we can also artifact content. And what we want to do is click on that object, that object is referred to as marked content in the context of PDF accessibility, and that marked content object, we do not want that to be read. And so we can right click on that object, and we have the same thing that we had in the Content pane. It's named a little bit differently though, this one says Change Tag to Artifact. And so if I choose that, we're going to get the same Create Artifact dialogue that we did in the Content pane,
>
> **[7:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/artifacting-elements-25577341?u=76281980&t=473)** but this time when we click OK, that object is going to disappear because it no longer will appear in the Tags pane when you make it an artifact. And now you can see this Figure tag is empty and it's no longer needed, so we can right click on that and simply choose Delete. Now again, if I go back to the Content pane and I look in Page 1, I will see, here it is, this object is in an artifact container. So when I artifact it in the Tags pane, that was reflected in the Content pane and properly identified the way we want it to be. So I usually save the artifacting step for the end of the remediation process. Once all meaningful content is tagged, you can identify non-tagged content in the Content pane and elements that require artifacting or have no value.

#### [Work with the Content pane](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/work-with-the-content-pane-25579381?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/work-with-the-content-pane-25579381?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The Content pane is a very powerful tool in Adobe Acrobat, but in my experience, it's very underutilized. In this video, I'd like to show you a few powerful ways that the Content pane can come in handy when fixing issues in a PDF file. Now, I'm starting this video with the 04_11 document open. And one of the things that I wanted to draw your attention to is that if you navigate to page two of this document, you might notice that the blue bars, there's no text where those blue bars were, as we saw in previous documents. And now you'll notice if you look at the reading order, it reads the main heading, this paragraph, this paragraph, this paragraph, then the figure, then the caption, and then it continues on to the next section, which is changes in policy. So not only does the Content pane control the stacking order of objects, it also affects the reading order. And just to give you a little bit of guidance here, we do have an Order panel over here in our Navigation pane. You do not want to use the Order pane to adjust the order of content in your document. I know it doesn't make sense.
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/work-with-the-content-pane-25579381?u=76281980&t=375)** You would think that the Order pane would be where you went to change the order, but if you modify things in the Order pane, it will obliterate your tag structure. So the beautiful thing about the Content pane is that it elegantly adjusts your stacking order and reading order, but it does not at all affect your tags. They remain where they are. So it's very powerful. It works very well. You know, we'll take a little bit of practice until you feel comfortable with it. > **[7:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/work-with-the-content-pane-25579381?u=76281980&t=467)** and reading order issues, and you're going to be able to get your document in really good shape.

#### [Add alternative text](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-alternative-text-25578362?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-alternative-text-25578362?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When a PDF document is read by assistive technology, inherently, images have no value. Images and graphics can't be read because they typically don't contain any real content. They're either made out of pixels or paths, and there's really no information to be garnered from those objects. To overcome this limitation, we as document remediators need to add alternative text to those graphics to describe their purpose. Now, this is a really good area where we need to talk about working with people on your team. I always say somewhat tongue in cheek, that as a graphic designer, I'm the worst possible person in the world who should be writing alternate text for graphics in a document. It's not really my skillset, it's really not my strength. And there are other people who are much better suited to writing alternate text for images than me. And so really, somebody in an editorial role, an editor, an author, a copy editor, those are the people who really should be writing this content. So you may want to try to work with other people and at least have them write the content for you. And then you can of course add that to the document when appropriate. So here I am with my Two Trees Olive Oil document open again, and I want to talk about adding alt text to images. And there's a couple of ways
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-alternative-text-25578362?u=76281980&t=92)** that we can do this inside of Adobe Acrobat, one of which is using our reading order panel. So let's go ahead and come up here to the All Tools button, and I'm going to choose Prepare for accessibility, and then Fix reading order. And when you turn on the Reading Order panel, you can see that all of the objects on the page that are tagged are showing up in kind of a gray box here. But way up here at the top, we have our figure, and you could see when we're in the Reading Order mode, in the Reading Order tool, the text here says Figure, no alternate text exists. #### [Add bookmarks](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-bookmarks-25581230?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-bookmarks-25581230?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] WCAG doesn't require bookmarks in documents, but WCAG acknowledges that adding bookmarks can improve the experience for users reading PDF files. This is found in WCAG's Success Criteria 2.4.5 Multiple Ways. It's also found in PDF Techniques for WCAG in item number PDF 2. And the PDF U/A specification indicates that bookmarks are not required, but they are in fact recommended. So for those documents, you should add bookmarks to assist users in navigating the documents. I prefer to add bookmarks to a document towards the end of the process for reasons I'm about to show you. Bookmarks are described in WCAG's Success Criterion 2.4.5. So I'm going to jump over to my Two Trees Olive Oil document, and I'm going to begin by opening up my Bookmarks panel, which is located over here on the right side of your screen. And the Bookmarks panel is this icon right here. If I click on that, it's going to open up my Bookmarks panel. And feel free to resize this to a size that makes sense for you. Now, one way we can add bookmarks is manually. So, whatever page you're currently on, if you create a bookmark, it's going to capture that appearance, and that's going to be where the bookmark takes you. So, I'm going to come up here and click on the Add a new bookmark button, and I'm going to call this Two Trees
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-bookmarks-25581230?u=76281980&t=94)** Olive Oil Employee Manual. There we go. And then I'll go to page two. Here's my table of contents. I can click the New bookmark button and I can type Contents. Now the way these bookmarks work is that if I click back on the Two Trees Olive Oil Employee Manual, it's going to take me back to that page, and if I click on the Contents bookmark, it's going to take me to that page. Now, that's easy enough, but this is a 22-page document, 21-page document, and I really don't want to have to manually go through the entire document adding the bookmarks manually. That's a lot of work. So I'm going to go ahead and delete these bookmarks. And one of the best ways to create bookmarks is to create bookmarks based on the structure of your document. Now, what I mean by that is the tag structure, and Acrobat gives you the ability to do this. And for this reason, I tend to add my bookmarks towards the end of the process after I've tagged all the content in my document, making the bookmarks is a breeze. So the way that this works is in the Bookmarks panel, I'm going to come up here to this three-dot menu, and I'm going to choose New bookmarks from Structure. Now, typically your bookmarks, you want them to be based on your headings. Those are the key areas of the document that you want to be able to navigate to. >
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-bookmarks-25581230?u=76281980&t=285)** which there are quite a few here. And this allows you to really nicely organize your bookmarks in a way that is a lot easier for users to navigate through them. And now you have all of these sections within your document that you can navigate, and you have a hierarchy established. So, really nice to navigate through, and if I click on Non-Disclosure Confidentiality, it takes me right there. If I go to Bulletin Boards, it takes me right there. The bookmarks are really, really helpful, and really easy for users to use. #### [Tagging footnotes](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-footnotes-25578360?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-footnotes-25578360?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Chad Chelius] Footnotes contain references as well as the footnotes themselves, both of which need to be tagged appropriately. We can find the tags needed in the reading order panel, which is very convenient, and I'd like to show you how to tag them here now. Now, if we navigate to page 21 in our Two Trees document here, you'll notice at the bottom of this page we have a footnote. Now if you look a little bit closer in the text of the document, you're also going to notice that we have a reference, which is located right here. So to tag this content, what I'm going to do is I'm going to open up my reading order panel. So I'm going to go to my prepare for accessibility tool, click fix reading order. And I got to be a little precise here because what I want to do is I want to select simply the number one in the text there. And you may need to try a couple of times till you get it, but basically I just want that number one to be selected. So to tag that number, we can just click on the reference button here, and you're going to see that that now has tagged that item as a reference tag. Now I'm going to scrub down a little bit further and I'm going to highlight the text of the footnote itself. And then I'm going to click on the note button to tag that item as a note. Now I'm going to close this,
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-footnotes-25578360?u=76281980&t=91)** and then let's take a look at our tags pane to see what has happened here. So I'm going to select this text and I'm going to choose find tag from selection. And you'll notice here that within the paragraph tag, so here's the paragraph, and within the paragraph tag, the reference tag is a child of that paragraph tag. Now the note tag, traditionally what we do here is we'll traditionally position the note tag immediately after the reference. You know, footnotes have inherent limitations, and this is certainly one of them. One of the things that I personally don't like about footnotes is that the user is forced to read the note when they're reading the content. So they don't really have an option of to whether to read the note or not, they're just going to read it regardless. But this is how we typically structure footnotes because the reference is tagged within the paragraph. So it's going to say under normal working conditions one, and then that's tagged as a reference, and then immediately below it is the [[Microsoft Word|word]] note. Now, one of the techniques that my partner, [[DAX]] Castro and I have established when it comes to working with footnote references, is we like to put the reference in brackets. So I'll try to show you what this would look like. I'm going to go to my edit button here
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-footnotes-25578360?u=76281980&t=188)** and I'm going to type a bracket. There we go, there we go. And what I just did, I want to warn you, using the edit tool in Acrobat can often cause very bad things to happen. Now in this case, it worked out pretty well. I find that if you're making an edit and you are not forcing rewrapping of lines and stuff, you can often get away with it. But if you are causing rewrapping of lines, it has a tendency to obliterate the tag structure in the document. So not a really great situation. But so we like to use brackets, and the reason we like to do this, the brackets there, is because when this is voiced using assistive technology, it's going to say under normal working conditions one, that's all it's really going to say. It's not going to tell you it's a reference, it's not going to identify it. It's just going to say under normal working conditions one. And what we found is if that reference ever occurs after another number, it's going to voice it as one giant big number. So if a sentence end in 2025, and then you had a one reference, it would voice it as 20,251. So the meaning would not be understood, it wouldn't make any sense. So by putting brackets it'll say open bracket one, close bracket.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-footnotes-25578360?u=76281980&t=282)** That way it separates it from whatever text is coming before and after it. And I have a little bit of a problem here, that comma is wonky here in there, but I just wanted to kind of show you what it looked like. And basically this is how you want to tag your footnotes. You tag the reference as a reference tag, the note as a note tag, and that will satisfy the requirement for footnotes. Now that you've tagged your footnote appropriately, your document is well on its way to full compliance.

#### [Walking the tags tree](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/walking-the-tags-tree-25578359?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/walking-the-tags-tree-25578359?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] As useful and helpful as automated checkers are, they are far from perfect. For this reason, there's a step of the remediation process that only we as humans can perform and that step is called walking the tags tree. This step basically consists of a human walking through the tags tree to ensure that objects are tagged appropriately and also that the tags are being read in the correct order. A checker can tell you if a figure is missing alternate text, but it doesn't know if texts should be a heading, or if elements are out of order. Let me show you how to walk the tags tree in Adobe Acrobat. Now I'm going to come over here to my tags pane and I'm going to open it up, and as I told you before, you do have the ability to make these tags bigger if you wish. You can hold down the control key on your keyboard and use the scroll wheel on your mouse, or you could press control plus and minus to increase or decrease the size of those tags. So I'm going to make my tags a little bit bigger just to make it a little bit easier for you to see as I'm walking through my tags tree. So I'm going to start by clicking on the figure tag way up here at the top of my tags tree and it's important for us to understand that both JAWS and NVDA, two of the most prominent screen readers in use today, use the tag order as the order in which content is read in a document. So after clicking on the figure tag, I'm simply going to tap the down arrow key on my keyboard and that's going to show me the next item that's going to be read and I'm just going to continue tabbing through here,
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/walking-the-tags-tree-25578359?u=76281980&t=97)** hitting the down arrow key and taking a look at the order in which my content is being read, and I'm just ensuring that everything is being read in the order that I want it to be read and so here's a situation where my figure and caption are being read kind of late on the page, so I'm going to move them up so that they're read here and then it'll read the section at the bottom. That's the whole point of walking the tags tree is to identify those issues that you find and so I'm just going through my document. When I get to like my list, one of the nice things about walking the tags tree is you could tap the right arrow key to open a tag and I can keep drilling down to kind of look inside to see what's inside of these tags, and then the left arrow will close the tag. So again, I'm walking the tags tree, making sure that as I go through my document that everything is being read in the correct order, and then I'm also checking my headings, making sure they're tagged as headings, making sure they're tagged appropriately and just kind of making sure that everything is being read the way that I want it to be. Here's another situation where the figure and caption's being read a little bit late, so I'm going to move that up so that it's read after this paragraph and then it'll continue from there. So again, the whole point of walking the tags tree is identifying those situations. So we're getting through our document here.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/walking-the-tags-tree-25578359?u=76281980&t=192)** Here we have another figure and caption. I think I'm okay with, with the order that that's being read. Yeah, that's looking pretty good. Now the one thing I want to show you while we're walking our tags tree, and as we were tagging our document, it's something that we weren't really paying attention to, but as a standard practice, all of the tags need to live inside of a document tag, and currently that document tag is missing. So what I'm going to do is I'm going to click on this tag up here. I'm going to right click and say new tag, and from the list I'm going to choose document and I'm going to move that up to the top, and then I'm basically going to click on the first tag, shift click on the last tag, and that's basically going to select all the tags in my document, and then I'll scroll up to the top and I'm just going to drag these highlighted tags to nest them underneath my document tag, and it takes a couple of seconds, 'cause we're moving a lot of tags in this document and we're kind of rearranging all those tags, but in a couple of seconds, you'll now see that all of my tags live under the document tag, and so as you can see, walking the tags tree, this is a crucial step and it's not hard, but it's an important step to ensure that the experience for a user of assistive technology is equal to the experience of any other user.

#### [Validate the PDF file and cleanup](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/validate-the-pdf-file-and-cleanup-25581231?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/validate-the-pdf-file-and-cleanup-25581231?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] As a final step in the process, we'll rely on automated checkers to verify compliance and to address any issues that remain at this point. So let's check this document and see where we stand. So I'm going to come over here to the all tools button, and I'm going to choose prepare for accessibility, and I'm going to choose check for accessibility. Now, I know I mentioned previously that one of the shortcomings of the Acrobat Checker is that it does not check to any standard, but that doesn't mean it's not useful. Even though it doesn't check to any standard, it does check for any issues that could cause significant barriers in your document. So I always like to start with the Acrobat Checker and address any issues there, and then we can move on from there. So I'm going to go to the page content area and I can see that I've got some tagged content that is failing. And if I look at those errors, you can see that essentially if you remember when I tagged this table of contents, I intentionally did not tag the dot leaders, and because of that, I'm now getting an error that that is untagged content. And then of course, the footers at the bottom of every page are also untagged and need to be artifacted. So the easiest way to do this is to come over to my content pane, and I'm going to come up here to page two. And the dot leaders really stick out here; we can identify them pretty easily. And what I'm going to do is I'm just going to control + click to select all of the dot leaders on this page. Because the content pane is organized at the page level,
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/validate-the-pdf-file-and-cleanup-25581231?u=76281980&t=97)** I can only do a page at a time, but I'm going to grab all of these and once I have them selected, I'm going to right click and choose create artifact, and that's going to artifact all of those items. You can see now in the content pane, they all have an artifact label attached to them. I'm going to do the same thing to page three here. And I'm also going to grab the path and the footer at the bottom, and I'm going to go ahead and artifact them as well. Now what I need to do at this point, and this is what's nice about the content pane, is that the objects that are untagged, they really stick out. Anything that doesn't have a container is untagged content. So I'm really just kind of going through each of these pages and I'm artifacting anything that is currently not tagged, and I can feel pretty confident that this is a good move because I basically tagged everything of importance on every page, and so because of that, I know that anything that's left over is pretty much good to go ahead and artifact it. So I'm just going to go ahead and do these pages here.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/validate-the-pdf-file-and-cleanup-25581231?u=76281980&t=178)** And on this page we have quite a few paths because these are all tables, and tables notoriously generate a lot of extra paths because every border of every cell in the table becomes a path, and those too, of course, need to be artifacted.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/validate-the-pdf-file-and-cleanup-25581231?u=76281980&t=204)** Here's another table. Again, bunch of paths.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/validate-the-pdf-file-and-cleanup-25581231?u=76281980&t=218)** And there we go. So now I'm going to recheck this for accessibility. And now when I look at this in my page content area, now I can see that all of the elements have been artifacted, so that's easy enough to address. It's a little time consuming, but fairly easy to address. Now the tab order, this is just something that's expected for accessibility. If a user uses their tab key to navigate through a page, they want that order to be correct, and this is an easy fix. We can right click on this error and choose fix. And basically what Acrobat's going to do is it's going to set the tab order to match the tag or structure order. So if your tags are correct, which they should have been from walking the tags tree, then setting your tab order is going to be good as well. Then up here under the document category, we have one error, and this is basically just a stamp that's on the file that says: Hey, I'm tagged. And where you can see this, if you come up here to this menu and go to document properties, you'll notice at the very bottom of the description tab, there's this little flag here that says tagged PDF, and currently it says no. And obviously this is a tagged PDF, so we want it to say yes. So again, we can right click on that error and choose fix. And now if we go back to the document properties, you're going to see that that tagged PDF flag says yes.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/validate-the-pdf-file-and-cleanup-25581231?u=76281980&t=314)** Now, there are two issues that are always going to appear under the document category. One is to check the logical reading order, which we should have done when we walked the tags tree. And the other one is to make sure that all of the content in our document meets minimum color contrast, and I'm going to show you how to do that in a future video. So I'm going to go ahead and save this. Let me save this as a finished version here. So now that we've passed the Acrobat Checker and we're free from any significant barriers, let's move on to the next video where we're going to run our document through the PAC Checker and check our document for compliance.

#### [Checking color contrast](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/checking-color-contrast-25578361?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/checking-color-contrast-25578361?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Ensuring that text elements in your document have sufficient contrast is a key requirement for ensuring that low vision users are able to easily read text in that document. Let's take a look at a few methods we can use to check color contrast in our PDF file. So beginning here with the 0419 document open and I have installed on my machine the Color Contrast Analyzer application. You can get this on the TPGI website. The Color Contrast Analyzer is a free tool and it's available for both Mac and [[Windows]] and it's a really useful tool for being able to evaluate the elements in your document. The way the color contrast analyzer works, you'll notice that there's a foreground color and a background color here. And so what you could do is use the eye dropper and you can pick up the color of text in your document. This is really tiny text here, but I'll go ahead and grab that. And then you can pick up the background color as well and it'll tell you right down here what the current color contrast ratio is. Now for text elements, the color contrast ratio for text that is 14 point and smaller is a 4.5 to one color contrast ratio. And for 14 point bold and larger, it's actually a three to one color contrast ratio. So in this particular case, you could see
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/checking-color-contrast-25578361?u=76281980&t=96)** that my ratio is much higher than that requirement. And also down here at the bottom, it's also telling me that at all levels, this contrast ratio passes. So I'm going to go to the next page in my document and here I want to test this color that I'm using against the background. And that looks pretty good. I think that was the same color that we just looked at. And again, I'm just going to go through my document and any other colors that I've used in this document. And I think pretty much those are the colors that I've used. I think I could safely say that all of the text in this document passes. Now the other thing I'd like to bring your attention is that when you use the pack checker under the WCAG check, it is checking color contrast as well. If you go to results in detail and you go under one perceivable and then 1.4 distinguishable, you could see that it is checking the contrast of text. Now that being said, I wouldn't rely on the PAC checker entirely because in my experience, I've seen situations where I either get like a false positive where it tells me it doesn't meet minimum color contrast, but clearly it does. And I've also seen situations
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/checking-color-contrast-25578361?u=76281980&t=191)** where it passes color contrast, but in reality in the document, it doesn't. So it's certainly nice to have this. It's nice to have a a passing WCAG report and it's good for it to tell you that all of your text meets minimum color contrast. But I would still tell you to manually go through your document to make sure that everything in your document is in fact compliant. So ensuring that your text elements have a color contrast between the text and the background of 4.5 to one in the case of smaller text or three to one in the case of larger text will ensure that all users can easily read that content in your document.

Well, that's a really good question and my answer is always, "Have you tested the document with a screen reader?" There's no way to verify the experience for the end user than to actually test and read the document using assistive technology. So if you're ever in doubt, test the document. #### [Accessible PDF on Windows vs. Mac](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/accessible-pdf-on-windows-vs-mac?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/accessible-pdf-on-windows-vs-mac?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Although the Mac and [[Windows]] version of [[Microsoft Word]] are made by the same company, they unfortunately don't behave in the exact same way, nor do they contain the exact same features. In this video, I'll show you methods to create accessible PDF files from [[Microsoft Word|Word]] on both the Mac and Windows platforms, but for the remainder of the videos in this chapter, I'll be using Windows due to the fact that the features are a bit more robust. There's two main ways that we can create accessible PDF files out of Word, depending on what software you have installed. Now, one way we can do this is by coming up here and going to the File menu and choosing Save As. And in the Format dropdown here, we're going to choose PDF. > **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/accessible-pdf-on-windows-vs-mac?u=76281980&t=96)** And if you do, you're going to notice up here in the ribbon of Microsoft Word that you have an Acrobat tab. >
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/accessible-pdf-on-windows-vs-mac?u=76281980&t=195)** Adobe Acrobat is now adding a paragraph tag to table rows that essentially include all the paths that make up the cell borders. >
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/accessible-pdf-on-windows-vs-mac?u=76281980&t=289)** So I'm just going to click OK but now I'm going to click on the Create PDF button. I'm now going to come back over to my Windows side and I'm going to open up those PDFs that I saved on the Mac. And just to kind of compare, you'll notice, the Mac save, when it comes to my tables does not add the P tag in the row as the Windows side did. And if I go to the PDF Maker that was make on the Mac, this method does add the P tag. So I do not have a definitive answer for you regarding which method you use. I think it's going to depend on the type of document you're working on and the intent that you're trying to get for your end results. So regardless of platform, you can create an accessible PDF file from Word on both Mac and Windows, use the method that works best for your platform and for your documents.

#### [Layout considerations and setup](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/layout-considerations-and-setup?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/layout-considerations-and-setup?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the things you'll hear me say over and over in this course, is that you need to use the features built into the source application that you're using in order to achieve success when creating accessible PDF files from those documents. In the case of [[Microsoft Word|Word]], you want to honor the natural top to bottom flow of content that Word uses. This will ensure that your content is tagged in the correct order when you export to the PDF. When it comes to images, it's important that you anchor the image to the text, and then you can use the layout button here to control how the figure or the image is going to interact with the text next to it. Within Word, I'm also going to recommend that you turn on a few things to help you and set you up for success. If you come over here to the home tab, I like to always turn on my hidden characters button, because to me, this gives me the Cliff Notes into the document. It really shows me how the content is created and helps me to identify issues that may occur when I'm exporting to an accessible PDF file. What I'd also like you to do is to display your styles pane. If you come up here to the main style section, I know mine's pretty narrow on this monitor, but in the lower right corner of the style section, go ahead and click on this button here. And that will display your styles pane
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/layout-considerations-and-setup?u=76281980&t=93)** over here on the right. Feel free to resize that if you wish to give yourself a little bit more room for your document. And finally, the other thing I'd like you to do is to display your navigation pane. If you come up here to the view menu, you'll notice there's a checkbox for your navigation pane. I'm going to turn that on because that allows me to see, in my case, where I've used headings in my document and the navigation pane also makes it easy for me to navigate my document because I can click on these different areas and that'll jump to that location within the document. You could see in this document here, that we have honored my recommendation of keeping the natural top to bottom flow. Basically all of my text is kind of flowing from top to bottom. That's going to help me with my tag order in the exported PDF and is going to allow me to achieve success when I export to the PDF format.
