#### [Styles and outline levels](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/styles-and-outline-levels?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/styles-and-outline-levels?u=76281980&t=0)** - Styles in [[Microsoft Word|Word]] are the magic glue that connects content in Word to the tag that will be applied to that content in the exported PDF file. Let's take a look at how to use, apply, and modify styles in Word for success when exporting to an accessible PDF file. What I'm going to do here is I'm going to scroll down a little bit until we get to some of the headings in my document. And we already have our styles task pane open over here to the right. And what I'm going to do is I'm going to click in this text here that says, section one introduction. And when I do that, you're going to see that the heading 1 style is selected, indicating that that style is applied to that content. Now, if I change the style, for example, if I choose something like Body Text, you could see that it's formatting that text as the style is defined. But if I now go back to my Heading 1 and I click on it, it's going to change the formatting to the formatting that that style is defined as. So styles not only make it super easy to consistently format text in your document, but when you leverage the default heading styles in Word, like we're doing here, the Heading one style in Word, automatically maps to an H1 tag in the PDF. And down here, the Heading 2 style automatically maps to
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/styles-and-outline-levels?u=76281980&t=96)** the Heading 2 style. So there's a lot you can do here, and you can really have a lot of control here. So for example, like, if I wanted to change the way this style appears, one of the things I could do is I can come over here to the right of heading 2, I can click on this little arrow and I can choose modify. And that's going to bring up my style definition where I can make adjustments. So I'm going to come down here and click on format and then border. And I'm going to change the color of this border. I'm going to go with something a little bit lighter, maybe something like that. And I'm going to change the border and the shading, I'm going to click, okay. And now when I click, okay, you're going to notice that all of those green borders are going to update to reflect that change. And see all of them get lighter. So that's another power of leveraging styles. Now, sometimes you can run into some trouble. So up here, you'll notice that they used a style called Title. Now that is not one of the default heading styles in Word, yet, the title should be a heading level one. So, in order to make sure that this title style is mapped to an H1 tag in the PDF, I'm going to click on the arrow and I'm going to choose modify. And if I click on the format button and choose paragraph,
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/styles-and-outline-levels?u=76281980&t=192)** you're going to notice up here, the outline level is set to body text, and that is basically a P tag in the exported PDF. Now, if I had opened up the Heading 1 tag, that would be mapped to level 1, and the heading to tag is mapped to level 2. So to get the title to map to an H1 tag, I'm going to change this to level 1. I'm going to click, okay. And then, okay, again. And now that title style is going to map to an H1 tag. Now, another thing I want to point out here, somebody, as you could see, put a hard return here. And what that means is that this line is going to export as an H1 tag, and this line is going to export as another H1 tag. So I try to encourage people not to do that. What I'm going to do here is I'm going to delete this return. And that's going to make the line very long, right? But what I can do is just right click here and choose paragraph. And if I increase my right indent to, like, maybe an inch and a half, now the paragraph is wrapping the way that I desire, but I'm not exporting it as two separate tags. This is now going to export as one tag. So I'm going to save this file. And I'm going to save this as a PDF.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/styles-and-outline-levels?u=76281980&t=289)** And when I opened this up in Acrobat, you're going to see that my title is tagged as an H1. You're going to see that all of my main headings are tagged as H1s. Here, I have my H2s, and all of my headings are now tagged appropriately. So as you can see, styles are of paramount importance when working in Word. By applying the appropriate styles to content in Word, you control the tag that's applied to that content in the exported PDF.

#### [Tagging tables](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-tables?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-tables?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Tables created in [[Microsoft Word]] naturally export well to the PDF format because they're automatically tagged appropriately in the PDF. So, let's take a closer look at how to define those header rows in [[Microsoft Word|Word]] for proper export to the PDF format. So that, of course, becomes a problem because we do, in fact, need a header row and if I go to the next table, we have the same problem. And, of course, if I were to run the Acrobat Accessibility Check, just as a quick initial check, we're going to see that under the Tables category, we do, in fact, have two errors that the headers in both of those tables are failing. So, I'm going to close this and go back to Word and we're going to navigate to the first table, and that is found under the Section Six, Benefits table. And what I'm going to do is I'm going to click within that table. Now, up here in the menus,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-tables?u=76281980&t=95)** I'm going to click on the Table Design menu and way over here to the left, you're going to have a Header Row checkbox and a First Column checkbox. Now, for formatting purposes, I'm going to apply my purple table style up here, just to make it look a little bit better. Really has nothing to do with accessibility, though. >
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/tagging-tables?u=76281980&t=193)** Now, because I made the first column a header as well, you're going to see that the first cell of every row is also tagged as a header. If I go down to the second table, we're going to see the same thing. We have the THead for that first row and all of the cells in that row, our header cells. But the TBody, because nothing was defined, are all table data cells. And of course, if I were to run my Acrobat Checker as just an initial check of this issue, you're going to see that all of the tables are now passing because the header rows have been properly defined. So, when you create a table in Word, just be sure to define the appropriate header rows in the table for proper output to the PDF format.

#### [Add hyperlinks](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-hyperlinks?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-hyperlinks?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Simply typing a link in a document isn't enough to make that link accessible in the PDF file. You need to actually define that text as a hyperlink in order for the link to be accessible in the exported in PDF. Let's take a closer look at how to properly add a hyperlink in [[Microsoft Word|Word]], that will be included in the accessible PDF file. I'm just going to go ahead and scroll down to page two, where in the last paragraph on that page, there is a hyperlink that goes to an email address. Now, this text, as it is typed, will in fact be auto-detected in Acrobat. And I want to show you this because I haven't done anything to this at this point in time. But what I'll do, is I'll go ahead and save it as a PDF. And I'll put this in the finished folder. And when I open this up in Adobe Acrobat, I'm just going to navigate down to that paragraph that contains that link. And if I open it up, you're going to notice that within the paragraph tag is simply what we call marked content, or you can call this the content object, but, basically all we have in there is text. Now, what confuses people sometimes if I grab my hand tool. You may notice that when I hover over this, it turns into a clickable link and you're like, oh, well, that's great.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-hyperlinks?u=76281980&t=94)** That must mean that it's already a hyperlink. Well, in reality, Acrobat has a feature where it will auto detect implicit hyperlinks. So basically any link or email address like this that is typed out and that it can detect as a link. It will make it clickable. However, that link is not accessible. So, it'll be useful for say a sighted user, but, for anybody using assistive technology, they're not going to be able to follow that link to its destination. So, to make it work properly, we're going to go back to Word and I'm going to highlight this hyperlink, and I'm simply going to right click on that link. And I'm going to choose link from the menu. Now, in this Insert Hyperlink dialog box, I can add a link, I can link it to a file or a webpage, a place in this document, or even an email address, which is actually what I'm going to choose here. I'm going to click on email address, and then the text to display is up here. And then I'm just going to re-type that, right. So I can type feedback@[twotreesoliveoil.com](https://twotreesoliveoil.com) and Word, but because of how we're creating this, it automatically knows to add the mail to protocol so that this becomes a link that will go to that destination. Now, you may also notice there is a button up here
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-hyperlinks?u=76281980&t=188)** for ScreenTip. And this is basically adding alternate text to that link for the ScreenTip text, we could type something like, email your feedback to Two Trees Olive Oil. Right, something like that. So I'm going to go ahead and click okay. And then I'm going to click, okay again. And Word automatically creates a hyperlink character style that it applies to that link. Now you could of course change that style if you want to, and if you don't want it to be underlined, or if you want it a different color, you know, you could totally do that, not a big deal. But, by defining that link properly, that's going to make a big difference in the exported PDF. So I'm going to save this file as the finished version of this. There we go. And then I'm going to save it as a PDF. And I'm going to go ahead and open that PDF, and once again, I'm going to navigate down to that paragraph and the difference now because of how I define this is that if I open up that paragraph tag, you're going to see that there is now an actual link tag inside of that paragraph. And if you open up that link tag, there is a link OBJR object, which makes that hyperlink accessible for users of assistive technology.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-hyperlinks?u=76281980&t=282)** And if I right click on that link and go to properties, I can click on this Edit Tag button. And I'm only doing this to show you the results of what we did. I'm going to open K-Array, the first Dictionary, the Object. And you're going to see that there is a contents entry that contains that alternate text that I added in the ToolTip button, within Word. So, Word is actually doing a really good job here because that contents entry, if you watch the previous chapter, we talked about how every hyperlink needs essentially an alternate description and that alternate description is achieved by adding this contents entry on that link tag that contains that alternate text. So it does quite a good job and this link is now fully accessible. So for any links in your document, be sure to properly define them as hyperlinks so that there'll be accessible in the exported PDF file.

#### [Artifacting objects](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/artifacting-objects?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/artifacting-objects?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There will be times sometimes quite often when there are elements on a page that you do not want read by assistive technology. One case can be one, a decorative image or graphic is used in a document. But another more common case is when you have running headers or footers in a document at the top or bottom of each page. Both of these situations require the content to be artifacted. Let's take a closer look at how to do that in [[Microsoft Word|Word]]. I don't actually have any figures to mark as decorative here, but to show you, if I click on a figure here in Word you'll notice in the Alt Text task pane, there is a mark as decorative checkbox at the bottom. So if I were to turn that on, that would cause this figure to be artifacted and it would not be read by assistive technology. Now, another more common situation that you could run into, if you look at this document, you'll notice that I have a header that appears at the top and a footer that appears at the bottom of every page. And those headers and footers need to be artifacted. And the reason for that is because to users of assistive technology, the concept of a page is not nearly as relevant as it is to a sighted user. And that's because as we've been learning the content in an accessible PDF file is read based on the tag structure in a very linear fashion.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/artifacting-objects?u=76281980&t=97)** And it's not as relevant to know when they're leaving one page and beginning another one. And to have those headers and footers read on every page would actually be disruptive. So here in Word, we have leveraged the header and footer feature. What you're going to notice is if I double-click on the footer down here, you can see that I am now in the header and footer editing mode where I can either edit the header at the top or of course the footer at the bottom. I'm just going to go ahead and update the date for demonstration purposes here. And it looks like this page number is a little low to me that just bothers me a little bit. I'm going to bump that up. I think I would have built this in a slightly different way, but I think it'll work for what we're doing. And so when you leverage the header and footer feature in Word, those elements are automatically artifacted and they are not read by assistive technology. So to kind of show you this, what we can do, I'm going to come up here and close the header and footer editing mode. That way we're kind of back in the normal document mode. And you can see the footer has been updated on every page. And what I'm going to do here is I'm going to save this as a PDF. Now, when I open this PDF file in Acrobat and I go to the tags tree, what you're going to notice is that if I, as I start walking the tree here, right? It's going to read all the content, but you'll notice it's skipping over the header and footer.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/artifacting-objects?u=76281980&t=192)** Notice those header and footer elements are not being read on any of the pages. And probably a more accurate way of seeing that is if I were to go to the content pane in this case, I'm going to go to page five, since that's where I am. You'll see that there's a container artifact for the Two Trees olive oil. And you'll also notice that there is a container artifact for the information down here at the bottom. So by using that header and footer feature in Word, it has automatically artifacted those elements so they are not read by assistive technology. So when you have elements on a page that you don't want read, use the mark as decorative checkbox in the Alt Text task pane, but for running headers and footers leverage the header footer feature in Word to ensure that those elements are artifacted appropriately when exporting to the PDF format.

Now, some of these other issues, we can address using the preflight panel. I'm just going to do a save as on this and I'm going to call this finish final, open up our preflight panel, okay. So, the preflight panel is going to allow us
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/touch-ups-after-conversion-from-word?u=76281980&t=270)** to address a lot of these issues. Again, I'm in PDF standards, clicked on the wrench. And first thing I'm going to do is embed my fonts. So, I'm going to click on that, fix it and I will just replace the previous version of the file. We'll double check that then. I'm also going to scroll down a little bit here, I'm going to create the content entry for my link annotations, going to fix that one, go back to my profiles. And then I want to set the IDs for my notes and we should find that way up here under document, add unique ID to note elements, going to go ahead and fix that one. All right, so let's see how that did. I'm going to switch back over to my PAC checker. I'm going to run the final version of that through the checker and you can see now that a lot of these errors have gone away. There's one more that I forgot to do and that is to add the PDF UA identifier. So, that is down here, so, I'm going to run the set PDF UA entry and then let's check that again okay, cool. So, my fonts have been embedded, I addressed the metadata. Let's look at what else we have here. So, we have a figure with no bounding box and we have a figure inappropriate use.

> **[6:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/touch-ups-after-conversion-from-word?u=76281980&t=367)** So, I am going to address that issue in the next video, because it's a funky one and it's a fix that I think you guys might appreciate a different approach to addressing the problem. #### [Funky fix-up](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/funky-fix-up?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/funky-fix-up?u=76281980&t=0)** - All right guys. I decided to do this video as a bit of a reality check for you as well as for me, because to be honest, sometimes you run into funky issues that require you to think outside of the box. Let's take a look at this last error we're getting and see the approach that I take to address this really kind of funky problem. This is actually a PDF file. So we've already made the PDF from [[Microsoft Word|word]] in the previous video and we're addressing the remaining issues that we have to deal with. Now what I'm going to do, is I'm going to switch over to my PAC checker, and I'm going to run the file that I have open through the PAC checker. And you're going to see that I'm left with two errors in here. So if I look at the results in detail, they're both actually related to the same thing. They're both figure errors. And the one is, we're missing the bounding box. And then the other error is inappropriate use. And that's one of those cases where it needs the placement attribute applied. Now of those two errors, the biggest one is the bounding box. And we did cover this in a previous video, where if we come over here to the PDF and we click on the figure tag, and we right-click and choose properties, if we click on edit attribute objects, there is no bounding box attribute in here. So we need to create it. There's no easy way to do this unfortunately, so what I showed you in a previous video,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/funky-fix-up?u=76281980&t=93)** is if you right-click on the figure, I'm going to go to properties and I'm just going to copy the alt text, that way I don't have to re-type it. But basically what we did previously is we said, let's artifact this figure and re-tag it. So if I right-click on the path, choose change tag to artifact, click OK. I can now delete this empty figure tag. And now I can re-tag the figure. So if I come up here to the accessibility tool, open up my reading order panel, I'm just going to marquee this figure, and I'm going to click on the figure button to tag it as a figure. Now when you do this, you get a result that you're not exactly expecting. I have no idea why it happens, I kind of dove into the original tag and tried to take a look at it, and it's just something funky. That's all I can really say. And trust me, you're going to run into these issues yourself. So what I'm going to do and what I discovered to address this problem, I'm going to revert this file to the saved version. And the way I got around this, and it was just kind of a creative way of addressing this problem. I came over here to the edit PDF button, and I typically discourage people from doing this, because going into edit PDF is a sure way to mess up your tag structure. So what you don't want to do is come in here and start editing texts, because you're going to find your tags are going to go wonky. But in the case of the figure, it's a problem to begin with.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/funky-fix-up?u=76281980&t=188)** So what I did is I clicked and dragged to marquee this figure, and I simply right-clicked on it and chose cut. So let's just kind of cut it to the clipboard. And then I right-clicked again, and I did a paste to just kind of paste it back in there. Now I'm going to close editing mode, and if you come over to the figure now, you can see that there's really nothing in that figure tag. If you click on the content object, it really just doesn't exist anywhere. So I'm going to right click on that, and I'm going to change that to an artifact and I'm going to delete this figure tag. And now you'll find if you go into your reading order panel, and you tag this figure now, when I click the figure tag, now it tags the figure appropriately, and is not all wonky the way it was. Now if I close my reading order panel, you're going to find that the figure tag for whatever reason, ended up all the way down here in the structure. So I'm just going to drag that up to the top where it belongs, and just maybe walk my tags' tree a little bit. Another thing to point out, is whenever you put empty returns in word, they equate to empty P-tags in the PDF file. And you'll notice that none of the checkers, really identified any of them as errors and fundamentally they're not, but as a final cleanup process, I usually go in and will artifact those empty tags. But honestly the best way to avoid them,
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/funky-fix-up?u=76281980&t=285)** is to simply not use them in word. Instead you can use the space before and space after feature in word to achieve vertical spacing without an empty return. So these paragraphs that I've selected are empty now, I can just go ahead and delete them. And we still have one error on that, which is the inappropriate use. If I go to that figure, right-click and choose properties, I'm going to paste that alt text back in there, which apparently I lost. So I'm going to go ahead and just type it. And then if I go to the edit attribute objects, the BBox attribute has been added, but because this is the first tag within the document tag, it needs to have the block attribute applied. So if I click on this attribute object one, choose new item. I'm just going to type placement block, and I'll just save my document and let's go ahead and rerun that through the PAC checker. And now you'll see that all issues have been addressed, we can view the results in detail, and you can see that all issues have been addressed for the WCAG standard as well as the PDF/UA standard. Things don't always go as planned, in cases like these you have to get creative to achieve the desired result, and just basically try some things to avoid the error. Don't be afraid to try something new,
>
> **[6:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/funky-fix-up?u=76281980&t=377)** to get the results that you need.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/defining-powerpoint-tag-order?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Once you have the headings established in your document, you now need to make sure that you order of those tags is correct in the exported PDF file. So there's a couple ways we could deal with this. What we could do is we could simply leave this span tag in here that has all of the paths. So I can right click and go to properties, and we can simply change that section tag, which by the way is really a chart tag. We can just change that to a figure. Now, what we would want to do, probably just for precaution is I would probably want to go in here and artifact all of these items. And I may do that when I'm finished here but this way, we have a figure that has the alternative text that we read appropriately. Now, let's look at another example down here and again, the reason why these are not tagged as figures is simply because of how it was created in PowerPoint. You and I don't always get to control that. A lot of times, these files are given to us, they were created by somebody else. And so in this particular example here, I may take a slightly different approach. And the approach that I might take here is that fundamentally, each one of these elements is actually text, right? This one, we don't really know what that is. That's the one that's just kind of like random there. Here we also have the text elements. So what we could do
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-alternative-text-in-powerpoint?u=76281980&t=295)** is we could treat this not as a figure at all. What I could do is I could grab all of these P tags, pull them up and out of that section, and then simply make sure that they're read in the correct order. So for example, I would want this to read olive oils and then 54%. Then I'd want it to read infused olive oils at 37%. And then, of course, gift baskets, that is at 5%. And then finally, miscellaneous, that is at 3%. And then we have this random number down here. But essentially, now it's not going to be read as a figure at all. It's going to read sales by product, olive oils, 54%, infused olive oils, 37%, gift baskets, 6% and miscellaneous, 3%. And then down here, I mean, these are all empty tags at this point. We can simply delete those. And then the remainder of these are just shapes that we can come in here and select those content objects, and change the tag to an artifact. So I kind of combined a couple of techniques in this video but it's honestly just kind of a great way of leveraging what you have. We could either try to describe that
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/add-alternative-text-in-powerpoint?u=76281980&t=392)** with alternative text but a better experience is to simply have text read appropriately. So a couple of different options that you have there. So fundamentally, be sure to add alternative text to figures in PowerPoint to ensure compliance in those accessible PDFs but based on how the document is created, you may want to make some modifications to create a better [[User Experience (UX)|user experience]] for the end user.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/work-with-powerpoint-tables?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] [[Microsoft PowerPoint|PowerPoint]] has the ability to insert tables onto slides in a presentation, but you now know the requirement of tables is that they contain header cells. Let's take a look at how to define header cells in a table in PowerPoint. I'm going to go ahead and come down here to slide four. Now, I think we saw in a previous video, we had an issue with this table because even though visually it looks good, it didn't have any header rows. So to add header rows to this table, what I'm going to do is I'm going to click inside of this table, and up here, I'm going to click on the Table Design menu, and very similar to [[Microsoft Word]], you'll notice that over here on the left, we have the header row and first column checkboxes, right, so the header row is going to define this top row as a header row, and first column would define this first column as a header row. And in this particular table, I'm going to go ahead and do both. So I'm going to turn on Header Row and turn on First Column. Now I'm going to go ahead and save this as a PDF. And what we're going to find is that when we go to our tag structure and we go down to that table, if we open up that table, you're going to notice the first row. Every cell is defined as a header cell. And within each successive row, you'll notice that the first cell of each row is also defined as a header cell
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/work-with-powerpoint-tables?u=76281980&t=96)** so that the cell is now appropriately tagged as a header cell. You might remember from some previous videos, that does a really good job, but one of the issues that we do run into is that even though PowerPoint does a good job of tagging those cells as header cells, one of the things it's missing is the scope that is defined for those cells. So what I'm going to do here, I'm going to go to my accessibility panel and I'm going to open up my reading order panel and I'm going to click on one of these labels and I'm going to choose Table Editor and I'm going to go ahead and select all of these cells at the top. I'm going to right-click and choose Table Cell properties and I'm going to go ahead and apply the Column Scope to those cells to flag them as header cells for the column. And then I'll go ahead and grab all of these cells in the first column and right-click and choose Table Cell Properties and tell them that they are header cells for the row. And that's just going to ensure that for, WCAG and PDF/UA, that that table is defined properly. So, guys, whenever you use tables in PowerPoint to display data, be sure that you define the appropriate header cells for the table for full compliance.

#### [InDesign layout considerations](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/indesign-layout-considerations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/indesign-layout-considerations?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Tutor] Unlike programs like [[Microsoft Word]], which has a natural top to bottom flow, InDesign as a page layout application, doesn't restrict you in any way when it comes to how you lay out content on a page. Any layout can be made accessible, but certain layouts might require a bit more assistance in controlling how content is read. Let's look at a few options. You can see here on this first page, I have a couple of images here. And then we have a text train down here. Now, when order of content is determined in InDesign, by default it starts in the upper left hand corner, goes across, and then down. Depending how content is laid out on your page, that order may or may not be okay with what you're trying to achieve. Now, as I move down to some other pages, you'll notice here my table of contents is linked from one page to the other. And I really only have a single frame on each page. As I keep going, you can see that, again, for the most part, the content is flowing from page to page. Now, a document like this is actually very easy to control the order because the order that the content appears within the frame is going to be the order that it's going to appear when the PDF is exported. Therefore, the order that assistive technology
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/indesign-layout-considerations?u=76281980&t=93)** is going to read that content. When you are using flowing text in a document, it's actually quite simple to control the order. Now, I've also opened these 06_02b file. This of course is a tri-fold brochure. Now, with a document like this, I often ask the customer, would it make more sense to export this brochure as single pages because then it's a lot easier to control, certainly page order, but also your content order. Some clients are not happy with that and they still want it to look the way it looks here. Now, in a case like this, the challenge, of course, becomes that this is the front cover. And then in theory, the next page that is read is this page over here. And then this one, and then this one, back to here, and then the last page. Now, again, if I just export this without any assistance, the order is going to be completely incorrect. But InDesign gives us the ability to very precisely control the order. We're going to have to leverage that feature when we do a project like this. But I just wanted you to see some different scenarios and some different things to keep in mind as you are remediating your own documents,
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/indesign-layout-considerations?u=76281980&t=188)** because depending on how the document is laid out, you're going to have to take precautions and just make sure that your content is exported in the correct order. Please don't let accessibility dictate how you lay out your document, but do keep in mind how you'll control the order of objects when you do export to the accessible PDF format.

And in the case of bulleted and numbered lists, they need to be tagged as lists, which they will be tagged as such as long as automatic is chosen. So, I'm going to go ahead and click, okay,
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/indesign-styles-and-tag-structure?u=76281980&t=280)** now that I've done all my mapping, and I'm going to go ahead and save this version as my finished version, and then I'm going to go ahead and export this to a PDF. And when the PDF opens, if I come over here and I look at my tag structure, you're going to find that the employee manual is now tagged as an heading level one. The table of contents title is an H1. And then here's my H1 for section one, and as I go down, there's an H2, and you can see now that all of my text is now mapping to the correct tag. Creating styles and defining the export tags is a critical step in the remediation process, and helps us to meet WCAG success criterias 1.3.1, 2.4.1, 2.4.8, and 3.2.3.

#### [Anchoring objects in text frames](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/anchoring-objects-in-text-frames?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/anchoring-objects-in-text-frames?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you have a lot of flowing text in a document, or when you need to precisely position an element within a section of flowing text, the only way to get that element to appear at the proper location is by anchoring it into the text. Our Two Trees employee manual has this situation. So I'm going to walk you through how to anchor images into text for proper tag order. And I'm doing this because I want to show you that if you look at the tag structure here, you're going to notice that it reads all of the text in the document, and actually here, it goes all the way down to page 21. And then it jumps back to page four and tags the figure and the caption. And this is kind of strange, right? Because you're like well, why is it doing it that way? Well, the answer is actually pretty simple. I'm going to close this PDF and I'm going to go back to InDesign. And the issue we run into is that from page four here, all of the text flows from page to page, all the way to page 21. And so what happens is it tags all of the text within those flowing frames, and then it comes back and tags anything that it missed. And to be fair, the articles panel isn't really much help here because even with the articles panel, I can only put this figure and caption before the first frame of the threaded frames or after the very last one.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/anchoring-objects-in-text-frames?u=76281980&t=94)** So it's really not helping us at all. #### [Work with tables in InDesign](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/work-with-tables-in-indesign?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/work-with-tables-in-indesign?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Tables created in InDesign are handled quite well when exporting to an accessible PDF file. Where some programs struggle with things like merge cells, InDesign handles them beautifully. The main requirement for accessible tables is defining a header row in each table. So let me walk you through how to do that in InDesign. I'm going to go ahead and navigate to page 17 of this document where I have a couple of tables that have been created. Now, as a reminder, the tables on this page, a lot of people may try to build all of this content as one big table and the reason that that's not a recommended approach is because you've got a heading for dental insurance, life insurance and retirement plan that occur not at the top of the table. So it's very difficult to define them as headers midway down in a table. Now, for that reason, you're going to see that I created four separate tables. And by doing so, it allows me to kind of isolate each table and tag the tables individuals. Now, I'm going to start up here with health insurance. We have this top row, health insurance, and then in this table, we have a secondary header for PPO and HMO. To identify those rows as a table header, I'm going to highlight both of those rows,
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/work-with-tables-in-indesign?u=76281980&t=94)** and I'm going to come up here to my Table menu in InDesign, and I'm going to choose Convert Rows, To Header. Now, you're going to notice when you do that, the formatting of those tables changes, and that's because there are table styles applied to these tables. And by nature of converting the row to a header row, the table style formats that header row in a unique way. So I'm going to go ahead and do the same thing to dental insurance. I'm going to go to Table, Convert Rows, To Header. I'm going to do this to life insurance. And then finally, to retirement plan. Now, if you advance to page 19, there's one table on this page and we're going to do the same thing. We'll highlight that top row and we'll go to Table, Convert Rows, To Header. Now, InDesign, although it allows me to define a column header at the top of a table, I cannot define row headers in InDesign. And that's simply a limitation of the product. We certainly could make that change in Acrobat after the PDF has been created but there is no way natively in InDesign to define this first column as a header row for the data going across. So I'm going to go ahead and save this file as the finished version. And then I'm going to go ahead and export this to a PDF.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/work-with-tables-in-indesign?u=76281980&t=198)** So if I jump to page 17 now, I'll go ahead and use my little trick here. I'm going to grab my selection tool. And I'll highlight this paragraph above and I'll choose find tag from selection. And that's going to allow me to quickly locate those tables. Now, one of the limitations of InDesign, because of how tables are structured within the InDesign application, what you're going to run into is that tables are always nested inside of a P tag. Now, the PAC checker is going to identify that as a problem. It does not like the table nested inside of a P tag. So one of the things that I always do after I export the file is I will simply pull those tables out of those P tags so that they simply reside as the children of basically the document tag, if you will. And I can now delete those empty P tags and then let's go ahead and do the same thing to the one on page 19 here. And that's pretty much it. Now, the one other thing I'm going to point out, if we were to run this through the PAC checker and test this, the first row, you could see that our table is broken up into a T head and a T body. And, of course, all the cells of that header row are TH tags, which is perfect. What InDesign is not able to do, even though it defined the row as a header,
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/work-with-tables-in-indesign?u=76281980&t=291)** it was not able to define the scope of the table. And so for that reason, I need to do that in Acrobat after export. So it's pretty simple to do. If we go to the accessibility tool and click on reading order, I'm just going to click on one of these numbers in the table, and choose table editor. And I'm just going to select these three cells by holding down the Shift key. I'm going to right click and choose Table Cell Properties and set the scope to be a column header. That's all you have to do. I'm going to go ahead and repeat that for these tables as well. One of the things that you'll notice, you'll notice that the column span is already defined. And that's one of the great things that InDesign is able to do for us when we export a table that does, in fact, contain merged cells. In this case, I'm going to select all four of these cells up there at the top, and I'm going to set them all to be column headers. So that is pretty much all you have to do. A little bit of cleanup on the backend but InDesign natively does a really good job of again, handling merged cells, identifying the header cells and we just have to add a little bit on the backend. So by properly defining your table header rows in InDesign, and some minor tweaks after export, you ensure that tables are compliant when exported to the accessible PDF format.

We also learned that those links are not accessible. So in order for links in our document to be accessible, we need to create an actual hyperlink for that text in InDesign so that the link is properly tagged in the export in PDF file. #### [Create a list with InDesign](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/create-a-list-with-indesign?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/create-a-list-with-indesign?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Bulleted and numbered lists need to be tagged correctly in an accessible PDF document to ensure that the experience is equal for users of assistive technology, as it is for all other users. To get lists to be tagged correctly, it's important to create them properly. Let's walk through how to do this in Adobe InDesign. I'm going to navigate to page 12 of my document. And at the bottom of this page, I have purposefully removed the bullets on these items so that I can walk you through the process of doing it on your own. Now, what I'm going to do here is I'm going to [[Zoom]] in on one of these bullets down here at the bottom. And the first thing I'm going to show you is the method not to use. Now I know there's a lot of users out there who have been doing this for years. They will come in here, click in front of the item, and they will type a bullet followed by a space and then they will use the indent to here character,
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/create-a-list-with-indesign?u=76281980&t=70)** to get the bullet to hang. Now, I'm here to tell you to stop doing this. And I'm talking to all of you former Quark users out there, because I know that that is where this comes from. So I'm going to undo all of that. And the right way to approach this is with your cursor inside of this paragraph. We're going to make sure that we're in our paragraph formatting over here, and I'm going to click on the bulleted list button up here in the control panel. That is going to add a bullet to that item. Now, I know what you're thinking. A lot of people do this and they're like, I don't like the distance of that bullet from the text. Well, that's not a problem. You're going to come back up here to the bulleted list button. You're going to hold down the alt or option key, and you're going to click on that button again. Because this is where we can adjust the spacing and the indentation of the bullet. So I'm going to reduce the first line indent to negative .125. And I'm going to increase the left indent to .3125. >
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/create-a-list-with-indesign?u=76281980&t=165)** to the selection and click okay. And now we can zoom out a little bit and I can highlight all of these items and apply my Bullet Point New style to them so that they all have the bullet applied. Now, this is a little bonus tip here because when it comes to bullets in an InDesign document, I've seen a lot of different approaches to this problem because what we often want is some additional space above the first bullet and some additional space after the last one. So now we're going to get a space before the first bullet, space after the last bullet, but no space in between them. And that is how you can maximize the way that bullets function inside of InDesign. I'm going to go ahead and save this as my finished version. And then I'm going to go ahead and export a PDF
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/create-a-list-with-indesign?u=76281980&t=262)** from this file. And in the PDF, I'm going to navigate to page 12 where I did those bullets. And if I go ahead and find my tag from selection, you are going to see that we have this beautiful list. Each item is its own list item within the list. And within each list item, the bullet is tagged as a label and the text is tagged as an L body. That is how you maximize lists inside of InDesign. Provided you create your list correctly, you should have good success ensuring those lists are properly tagged in the exported PDF file.

#### [Alternative text for images and graphics in InDesign](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/alternative-text-for-images-and-graphics-in-indesign?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/alternative-text-for-images-and-graphics-in-indesign?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Every image and graphic in a document either needs to contain alternative texts, to describe that image or be artifacted to prevent it from being read by assistive technology. #### [Adding actual text](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/adding-actual-text?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/adding-actual-text?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Similar to alternate text, actual text can be used as a direct replacement of an image for text. With actual text, content is voiced as if it were text instead of an image. This can, in some cases, be a better experience for the end user. Now I'm starting this video with the 07 16 document open, and the logo at the top of the page could arguably be a good example of where I might want to use actual text instead of alternate text. So what I could do here is I can click on the graphic at the top of the page, and I'm going to go ahead and go to the object menu and choose object export options. Now we're used to being here in the alt text category where we were adding alt text to our images, but if you click on the tagged PDF button, you'll notice that right here is a actual text source drop down. Now you can grab your actual text from [[Metadata]] fields as well if you wish, but you can also simply choose custom. And in this particular example, I might just want to type here, Two Trees Olive Oil, and that is going to apply actual text to that graphic instead of alternate text. And again, the difference is that this is going to become a direct replacement for the image and it's going to be read as text.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/adding-actual-text?u=76281980&t=95)** So when you click done, now we know that this graphic has actual text applied to it, and that's how that graphic is going to be voiced when read by assistive technology. So when appropriate, be sure to use actual text instead of alternate text for a better [[User Experience (UX)|user experience]].

#### [Artifacting InDesign objects](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/artifacting-indesign-objects?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/artifacting-indesign-objects?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] In some documents, especially ones that are highly designed, imagery and graphics are often used strictly for aesthetic reasons. Elements like these really don't serve any purpose in the document nor have any value. So for these elements, we can prevent them from being read by assistive technology, by artifacting them. Let's take a look at how to do that in, InDesign. I've decided that this large image on the front cover, maybe I don't really want it to be read. Maybe it doesn't really have any value in the context of the document. I mean after all, this is an employee manual. And so how useful is it for me to describe different flavors of olive oil in a document such as this? So if I wanted to artifact this, what I can do is, I'm going to come up here with this selected, to the object menu, and I'm going to choose object export options. And previously I've added Alt Text to this. So I'm going to delete that Alt Text and set the Alt Text source back to from structure, just so there's no conflict. And then I'm going to click on the tagged PDF button. And in that section, there's an apply tag menu. And if I click on the dropdown menu, I can choose artifact. And that is now going to tell this image to be artifacted and will not export. So I'm going to go ahead and click on done. And if I go ahead and-- I'll go ahead and save this as the finished version.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/artifacting-indesign-objects?u=76281980&t=100)** And then just for demonstration purposes, I'll go ahead and make a PDF of this. And you'll see in the tag structure, as I start clicking from the top, there's the first figure, and then it jumps right down to the text. Never is that figure in the middle of the page read. And you'll also be able to see this by going to the content pane and going to page one, you'll notice that this image is artifacted. So I'm going to go ahead and close that. I'm going to return to InDesign. And I'm going to go ahead and close this document. Now I also have a B version of this document, because I wanted to show you that another way that you can artifact an object, is to simply not include it when you're using the articles panel. So for example, I'm going to open my articles panel over here. And let me make this a little bit wider. You could see that there are many items on this page that are in fact in the articles panel. But if I click on this design in the background, you'll notice that it is not in the articles panel here. And by doing that, that item is automatically going to get artifacted. So once again as a quick demonstration, I'm going to go ahead and make a PDF of this.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/artifacting-indesign-objects?u=76281980&t=190)** And that was on page four. So in the tag structure, I'm just going to kind of bounce down here to page four. There we go. And so you can see on page four, it's reading the figure, and then it's jumping right to page five, right? Because the text, there we go. There's all the texts. And that'll take us all the way down to page four. So that text is being read. But then beyond that, it's only the figure. And that background object has been artifacted. If I take a look in the content pain on page four, that shape, which is hard to see, that is that shape there, has been artifacted. So for elements that serve no purpose in a document or don't have any value, using designs capability to artifact them, to improve the experience for users of assistive technology.

So when we built the Table of Contents, InDesign automatically went in and created bookmarks for every one of the headings that was defined within that document. So it is a really powerful feature. And so for a document like this, the bookmarks are already created, and there's not much more you need to do. Now, if for some reason there was a bookmark that you wanted to create that did not exist in here, you can always use the plus sign right here to create another bookmark. Now, I'm going to walk you through that process. Let's close this document, and I'm going to switch over to the 06_16b document. Now, this is what was the tri-fold brochure that I kind of split into individual pages. And basically the way bookmarks work
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/pdf-bookmarks-in-indesign?u=76281980&t=94)** is when you create a new bookmark, whatever your current page is, that becomes the bookmark destination. So what I'm going to do here is I'm going to click on the plus sign, and I'm going to call this Cover. Then I'm going to go ahead and go to the next page, and I'll create another bookmark, and I'll call this one Panel 1, go to the next page, call this one Panel 2. And as you might guess, we're going to call this one Panel 3. And then for the fifth page, this is what I would call the outside panel. And if I click on Panel 1, it goes to Panel 1, Panel 2, Panel 3, the outside panel, and the back cover. So you have individual items within the index that of course can be read and interpreted by assistive software. So by utilizing the Index feature in InDesign, you ensure that the index is tagged appropriately, and it's accessible in the exported PDF file.

#### [Finishing touches and validating](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/finishing-touches-and-validating?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/finishing-touches-and-validating?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right, well we've done everything that we can in InDesign to get the generated PDF accessible. So now we just have to do a little bit of post-export cleanup to reach full compliance. I just want to point out that I've already taken the steps to basically clean up the Tag tree. So I've pulled all of the Tags out of their containers to make it a little bit easier for me to see what I'm working with. And I've also walked the Tags tree. So I've done things like pulling the Tables out of their P tags. I cleaned up the Notes, all of which we've already covered in this chapter. Now I want to see where we stand and what's left to take it to the finish line. So I'm going to go ahead and close this document. And here I have these 06_19 file, and the PAC 2021 Checker open. I'm going to go ahead and drag this over here to run the file against the PAC 2021 Checker. And as you can see, we've got a couple of issues that need to be addressed here. If I click on Results in Detail, we can see immediately under Content, this is one of the areas where InDesign, unfortunately, doesn't do a very good job. I mean, every single Path, like every single vector object in this document is untagged, okay? So I know that looks like a scary number up here, but I assure you it's a very quick fix. We also have our Notes need IDs. Okay, that's another easy fix.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/finishing-touches-and-validating?u=76281980&t=95)** Our Tables, they don't have the associated subcells. That's another pretty easy fix. And then our links need Alternative Descriptions and we need the PDF/UA identifier added. Okay so what I'm going to do is, I'm going to reopen that file. And let's address the Table situation first. So the first group of Tables is on page 17. I'm going to go to the Accessibility tool, open Reading Order, and I'm going to click on one of these Labels in the Table, and I'm going to choose Table Editor. And I'm simply going to select all of the Header Cells. Then I'm going to right click, choose Table Cell Properties and set the Scope to be a Column Header. That's really all we got to do. Unfortunately, InDesign does not define the Scope on these Header Cells. And so that's something that I have to go in and do for each of these Tables. And then I have one over on page 19. Let's take care of that one as well. As you can see, this is a pretty simple and quick fix. All right, there we go. So I'm going to go ahead and Save that. Now, to fix some of the other issues I'm going to lean on the Preflight panel. So I'm going to go ahead and go up here to the Search Tools, I'm going to search for Preflight and I'm going to open my Preflight panel. Now again, you want to be in PDF Standards and click on the wrench icon, and now you can go in here and run some of these fix ups. So I'm going to click on Add Unique ID to Note elements.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/finishing-touches-and-validating?u=76281980&t=189)** I'm going to go ahead and run that. And I should probably Save this to my Finished folder. So let me rename this to the Finished version. Then I'll go back to my Profiles. The other one I'm going to run is Mark all non-structure elements as artifacts. So that's going to take all those paths and artifact them. Let's go ahead and run that one. Go back to our Profiles. I'm now going to scroll down a little bit. Let's go ahead and Set our PDF/UA-1 entry.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/finishing-touches-and-validating?u=76281980&t=229)** And way down here, under Interactive elements and properties, we have Create Content entry for Link annotations. So I'm going to run that one yet. Perfect. And now we can go back to our PAC Checker and I'll run this one more time and we should now see that this document reaches full compliance for PDF/UA as well as for WCAG. So with a little effort after export, you could take your accessible PDF file to the finish line for a fully compliant accessible file.

### Conclusion

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Next steps](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/next-steps?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/next-steps?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Chad] So I'd like to leave you with some resources for next steps you can take for upping your PDF accessibility game. And I'm going to start here with the PDF Accessibility [[Facebook]] group. This group is a very active group with about 1,700 members, and it's full of people who are willing to help you with your PDF accessibility questions. It gives you an opportunity to provide your insight, and provide answers to other people's questions, and just a really good place to find great information about PDF accessibility. The other website I want to direct you to is the WCAG 2.1 website, where you can learn about the specific details of the PDF accessibility requirements. I've referenced many of these in the early chapters of this course, and you can go through here, and really dive into exactly what is required to make a document accessible. In addition, there's a great PDF file called the Tagged PDF Best Practice Syntax Guide. And this is really a great resource that I referenced quite a bit when I'm working on documents, and if I have a question about how tags should be nested or how things should be tagged, it's a really great place to go, and a good reference to have. In addition, you can check out the Matterhorn Protocol. Now this is a little bit of a deeper dive into PDF accessibility,
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/creating-accessible-pdfs-14445392/next-steps?u=76281980&t=92)** but it is a good resource. I tend to reference it for very specific items. It's certainly not something you want to read front to back. At least I don't, but it is a very good resource that I really encourage you to have. Also the PDF/UA Foundation. Again, is another great foundation that is there to promote the PDF/UA format, the PDFA specification, and it's also by the way, where you can download the PAC 2021 Checker free of charge. Be sure to check out the AbleDocs website. AbleDocs offers PDF remediation services, and also makes some really great PDF accessibility [[Microsoft Products|products]] that I have showcased throughout this course. And finally, and this is an important one guys, make sure you check out the "Chax Chat Podcast." "Chax Chat" is a weekly accessibility podcast hosted by my colleague, [[DAX]] Castro and myself, and we essentially wax philosophical about all things, document accessibility. We answer questions. We showcase tips, as well as products to help you in your PDF accessibility journey.
